# KaNeXT Revenue Model: Institutional

**KaNeXT LLC | Confidential**

---

## Overview

This document presents the institutional revenue projection for KaNeXT LLC through the SFBC partnership across a five-year horizon. Year 1 is the Athletics-Only Agreement under which KaNeXT operates athletic programs autonomously and the partner institution retains academic authority. Year 2 forward is the expansion to a Full Institutional Operating Agreement under which KaNeXT operates institutional infrastructure, enrollment, platform deployment, and, beginning with the Miami Lakes campus acquisition, physical campus operations.

The model shows both sides of the partnership economics. KaNeXT captures operator economics from athletic revenue, tuition share, platform revenue, and campus operations revenue. The partner institution captures direct partnership payments, retained non-athletic revenue, enrollment growth, and institutional transformation. Both parties grow the pie together. The partnership only succeeds if both sides win.

The model scopes institutional revenue through the SFBC partnership. Platform revenue at scale across mandate institutions, intelligence licensing, bank revenue, and other KaNeXT revenue streams are documented in separate revenue model files and are not quantified here. This document is the institutional revenue story.

---

## Comparable Institutions

Three Christian universities demonstrate three positioning strategies that inform the SFBC trajectory. Each achieved scale through a different mix of athletic visibility, campus investment, tuition positioning, and online expansion. None had the combination of advantages SFBC has.

### High Point University

Premium positioning. United Methodist-affiliated institution describing itself as Christian by tradition and interfaith in vision. High Point grew from approximately 1,450 students in 2005 to 6,335 on-campus today. Tuition rose from roughly $16,000 to $45,312 for 2024-25, moving to $46,444 for 2025-26, with total cost of attendance reaching $67,986 and rising to $71,429 for Tier 1 housing in 2026-27. Over $3B in campus investment over 20 years. 88% of students receive institutional aid averaging $21,416. Membership in Big South Conference at Division 1 level. The model is prestige pricing backed by campus experience and athletic visibility.

### Liberty University

Scale positioning. Evangelical Christian institution founded by Jerry Falwell, theologically conservative. Liberty reached approximately 16,000 residential and over 124,000 online students. Tuition at $24,650 for 2025-26 with room and board of $14,604. Total residential cost of attendance around $40,924. Annual revenue exceeded $1.2B. The model is mass-scale residential and dominant online market position at middle-tier tuition. Liberty is the proof that evangelical Christian higher education can scale past 100,000 students through disciplined online operations.

### Grand Canyon University

Affordability and online scale positioning. Non-denominational Christian institution, originally Southern Baptist, operating as a nonprofit since 2018. GCU froze on-campus tuition at $16,500 for 18 consecutive years while growing from under 1,000 on-campus students in 2008 to approximately 25,000 on-campus plus approximately 98,000 online. Room and board at $10,658. Over $1.8B in campus infrastructure investment. The model is disciplined affordability, aggressive marketing, and massive online scale.

### What the partner institution Has That The Comparables Did Not Start With

HPU, Liberty, and GCU each took roughly 20 years to reach their current scale. SFBC begins from a different starting point with structural advantages none of those institutions had.

SFBC already has ABHE institutional accreditation. Title IV eligibility at the undergraduate and master's level is active. Students are eligible for Pell grants, Direct Loans, and VA benefits. No accreditation buildout required.

SFBC already has online enrollment infrastructure built around Populi, in place since 2012, operating at meaningful scale with international students from 43 nations. The platform for online growth exists.

SFBC is launching an MBA in Fall 2025 and a BSN in development. Academic program growth is already underway institutionally before the KaNeXT partnership begins.

SFBC has a 40-year Christian institutional brand in Broward County, inside the Miami-Fort Lauderdale-West Palm Beach DMA, one of the largest metropolitan markets in the country.

KaNeXT adds what the partner institution did not have on its own: a live institutional operating system, governed intelligence across admissions and retention, nationally-visible athletic programming, NIL infrastructure, platform financial operations, a path to Division 1 athletic membership, and capital to fund the transformation.

SFBC's evangelical Christian identity occupies a distinct position from any of the comparables. HPU operates from a Methodist-interfaith tradition. Liberty is evangelical but at mass scale and middle-tier tuition. GCU is non-denominational at affordability pricing. The combination of evangelical Christian identity, premium-tier positioning trajectory, and AI-native intelligence platform is a category that does not currently exist in the market. SFBC occupies uncontested ground.

The partner institution does not need to build what HPU, Liberty, or GCU had to build. SFBC needs to execute the partnership, let the athletic engine drive visibility, let the enrollment engine convert visibility to enrollment, let the tuition trajectory follow the prestige build, and let the Miami Lakes campus anchor the growth. The comparables took 20 years. The partner institution reaches meaningful scale faster because the foundation is already there.

---

## Assumptions Grounding The Model

The model rests on five operational assumptions. Each is grounded in observable conditions at the partner institution and in KaNeXT's current capabilities.

**Athletic visibility is immediate, scheduled, and national.** Year 1 men's basketball schedules 12 to 16 Division I guarantee games, targeting essentially the entire non-conference slate. Every game broadcast on ESPN+ through host D1 school media agreements. The schedule alone puts SFBC on national television a dozen or more times during basketball season at an institution with eleven hundred students.

KaNeXT plans to build a mid-major quality team that regularly beats NAIA opponents by 30 to 40 points. The intelligence system selects D1 opponents where SFBC has measurable personnel and system advantages, scheduling D1 games KaNeXT is competitive in or favored to win. The objective is not to play D1 schedules as sacrifices. The objective is to use the intelligence architecture to identify favorable matchups and schedule accordingly, converting the guarantee game slot into a real competitive opportunity.

One or two D1 wins generates national media attention. NAIA programs have beaten D1 teams before, and when it happens, the story gets real coverage. An NAIA Christian bible college beating D1 programs using an AI intelligence system is the specific story arc that drives coverage to SportsCenter, national sports media, and beyond the sports category into general interest media. The founder's coaching track record at Lincoln University Oakland, where a roster built with no scholarships and no infrastructure won back-to-back conference regular season titles and the GAAC tournament championship while scheduling six D1 guarantee games, demonstrates the program-building capacity at maximum constraint. SFBC removes the constraints.

**The enrollment engine operates from Day 1.** KaNeXT's enrollment intelligence architecture, covering retention, persistence, pipeline, and recovery, deploys in parallel with the admissions intelligence and NIL intelligence already live. Enrollment operates against the full lifecycle from admissions inquiry through graduation, not just front-end recruiting.

**The online program infrastructure scales what exists.** SFBC's Populi platform and online operations are scaled rather than rebuilt. KaNeXT adds Dipson AI advising, national marketing capacity, and enrollment engine optimization to infrastructure SFBC already runs. This is acceleration, not construction.

**The Miami Lakes campus anchors Year 3 onward.** Miami Lakes campus acquisition begins Year 2. Initial operations begin Year 3. Majority of operations move Year 4. Full operations Year 5. The campus is the physical manifestation of institutional transformation and the economic center of institutional revenue from Year 4 forward.

**The tuition trajectory follows the HPU pattern.** Athletic visibility plus campus investment plus brand build supports rising tuition. SFBC does not lead with tuition increases. Tuition rises as the institutional product rises. Scholarship aid scales proportionally so net tuition remains accessible even as sticker tuition rises.

**The Year 1 athletic scholarship structure contributes to direct tuition revenue.** Because NAIA Varsity scholarship equivalencies do not activate until Year 2 NAIA membership, Year 1 deploys strategic scholarships only where recruiting requires. Most Year 1 athletes receive Title IV federal financial aid plus standard institutional aid and pay tuition as enrolled students. Twelve teams at approximately 15-18 athletes per team produces approximately 180-220 athletes in Year 1 as direct tuition-paying enrollment. Year 2 onward the Varsity teams activate NAIA scholarship equivalencies while Development teams continue as tuition-paying rosters. The athletic enrollment contribution compounds over Year 1 through Year 5 as the sport portfolio expands.

---

## Enrollment Trajectory

Enrollment grows through channels detailed in the Enrollment Funnel Model: athletic enrollment (direct recruiting of student-athletes), residential non-athlete enrollment (driven by athletic visibility and brand build), online enrollment (scaled through the existing Populi platform and KaNeXT marketing infrastructure), and international and diaspora enrollment (the partner institution's existing 424 international students from 43 countries plus the global recruiting and VoIP-driven diaspora channel).

The table below aggregates those channels into the two tuition categories that drive the revenue model: residential (on-campus) and online (including online-delivered international and diaspora students). The residential number includes the portion of international and diaspora students choosing on-campus programs. The online number includes the portion choosing online delivery. Channel-level detail by athletic, brand-driven residential, online, and international/diaspora is in the Enrollment Funnel Model.

| Year | Residential (On-Campus, incl. intl/diaspora on-campus) | Online (incl. intl/diaspora online) | Total Enrollment |
|---|---|---|---|
| Year 1 | 1,500 - 1,700 | 400 - 500 | 2,000 - 2,200 |
| Year 2 | 3,300 - 3,700 | 2,300 - 2,700 | 5,600 - 6,400 |
| Year 3 | 5,400 - 6,000 | 4,900 - 5,700 | 10,300 - 11,700 |
| Year 4 | 8,800 - 9,800 | 8,000 - 9,200 | 16,800 - 19,000 |
| Year 5 | 12,800 - 14,200 | 11,800 - 13,500 | 24,600 - 27,700 |

The partner institution's current enrollment is approximately 1,100 students. Year 1 growth to approximately 2,100 students is driven by 180-220 student-athletes added directly through KaNeXT athletic programs across six Varsity sports plus development tiers, plus 400-500 net new residential students driven by athletic visibility and enrollment engine operation at the athletics scope, plus early online program growth through enhanced marketing infrastructure.

Year 2 through Year 5 reflects the compounding effect of sports expansion from 6 Varsity sports to 14 sports by Year 5, development tier expansion across every sport, athletic championships driving brand build, Miami Lakes campus coming online providing residential capacity, full online program marketing through KaNeXT distribution channels, and Full IOA expansion enabling enrollment engine operation institution-wide. By Year 5, the athletic program contributes approximately 700-900 direct student-athlete enrollments across 14 sports with multiple development tiers per sport (500 Varsity plus 200-400 Development across expanded sport portfolio, per the Enrollment Funnel Model), generating continuous content for KTV and continuous visibility for the institutional brand.

For context, HPU grew from 1,450 to 6,335 in 20 years. Liberty grew from approximately 4,000 in 1985 to 140,000 by 2024 through aggressive online scale. GCU grew from under 1,000 in 2008 to 123,000 by 2024. Year 5 projection of approximately 25,000 to 28,000 total students is a fraction of Liberty and GCU's eventual scale, achieved in a compressed timeframe because SFBC has advantages those institutions did not begin with: existing ABHE accreditation, existing online infrastructure through Populi, immediate national athletic visibility through the D1 guarantee game schedule, the intelligence platform, and the KaNeXT distribution network providing marketing reach without the hundred-million-dollar marketing budgets HPU, Liberty, and GCU had to build.

---

## Tuition Trajectory

Tuition rises as the institutional product rises. Miami Lakes campus, expanded academic programs, elite faculty, national athletic visibility, and the full KaNeXT operating system justify the premium tier the institution grows into. The table below shows residential (on-campus) undergraduate tuition. Online tuition is lower per student (typical online higher education pricing, serving cost-sensitive working adult and international audiences) and pulls the blended average materially below the residential figure during the scaling years; blended residential+online averages are reflected in the year-by-year revenue commentary.

| Year | Residential Tuition (UG) | Room / Board | Campus Phase |
|---|---|---|---|
| Year 1 | $7,740 | Athletic housing via master lease | Deerfield |
| Year 2 | $8,500 - $9,200 | Athletic housing plus limited general | Deerfield, Miami Lakes acquired |
| Year 3 | $11,000 - $12,500 | Growing general housing | Deerfield, Miami Lakes Phase 1 opening |
| Year 4 | $15,000 - $17,500 | Miami Lakes housing majority | Miami Lakes majority, Deerfield retained |
| Year 5 | $20,000 - $23,500 | Full Miami Lakes operation | Miami Lakes Christian HPU state |

Scholarship aid scales with sticker tuition to preserve access across income levels. Online program tuition follows market pricing at approximately $350 to $450 per credit hour with growth capacity as program reputation develops.

---

## Campus Trajectory And Revenue Ownership

Where students live determines which party captures housing, dining, and campus commerce revenue.

**Year 1 through Year 3: Deerfield Primary, Miami Lakes Build**

The partner institution operates from its owned 5.4-acre Deerfield campus. Academic operations, administrative functions, and most student housing run through existing partner institution infrastructure. KaNeXT operates athletic-specific cluster housing through master lease. The partner institution retains all non-athletic housing, dining, and campus commerce revenue.

Miami Lakes campus acquisition proceeds through Year 2 with Graham Companies. Phase 1 buildout during Year 2 and Year 3 includes athletic facilities, initial residential housing, initial academic buildings, and initial student life infrastructure. First students begin relocating to Miami Lakes in Year 3 as facilities come online.

**Year 4: Miami Lakes Majority, Deerfield Retained**

Majority of partner institution students operate from Miami Lakes. KaNeXT owns the Miami Lakes campus and therefore captures housing, dining, facility fees, parking, campus retail, and event revenue from all students at Miami Lakes. Deerfield operates as a satellite campus honoring the founding location, with the partner institution retaining Deerfield-scoped revenue there.

**Year 5: Miami Lakes Full Operation, Christian HPU State**

Miami Lakes is the primary campus operating at Christian HPU scale. KaNeXT operates the campus and captures the full campus economics. Miami Lakes hosts athletic programs at a level approaching or achieving Division 1 membership, with associated licensing, media rights, and prestige revenue.

**The campus ownership question is the single largest revenue lever.** When KaNeXT owns the campus, KaNeXT captures the housing and dining revenue at the scale HPU and Liberty demonstrate. HPU's 6,335 residential students generate approximately $134M annually in room and board alone. Liberty's 16,000 residential students generate approximately $233M annually in room and board alone. SFBC at 12,800 to 14,200 residential students on Miami Lakes generates room and board revenue in the $190M to $285M range annually by Year 5.

### The $1/Year Lease Structure

When KaNeXT acquires the Miami Lakes campus, KaNeXT enters into a long-term ground lease with the partner institution at $1 per year. The partner institution operates academically on a KaNeXT-owned campus without paying market rent, preserving SFBC's academic authority and institutional continuity while transferring the physical asset burden to KaNeXT.

Under this structure, KaNeXT captures housing, dining, facility fees, parking, campus commerce, and event revenue as campus operator. The partner institution captures academic tuition per the Full IOA split, preserves its Deerfield founding campus, and operates from a flagship facility at no real estate cost.

The $1/year lease is the mechanism that makes the partnership work cleanly. SFBC gets a real flagship campus without the capital cost. KaNeXT captures the operational economics that justify the infrastructure investment. Both parties align economically on the same flywheel: KaNeXT wins when SFBC wins, because KaNeXT's campus revenue depends entirely on the partner institution's enrollment success.

The structure mirrors the GCU-GCE precedent, with terms structured substantially more favorably to the partner institution than the GCU historical arrangement.

---

## Investment In Institutional Quality

The tuition trajectory from the partner institution's current $7,740 to the Year 5 target range is not a function of sticker pricing alone. It is a function of institutional quality that supports the premium. KaNeXT's capital deployment includes substantial ongoing investment in the academic institution itself, not only in athletic programs and campus infrastructure. Students and families pay premium Christian university tuition because they experience premium Christian university quality.

**Faculty investment.** Christian higher education historically pays faculty below market rates, which caps academic quality and program reputation. KaNeXT invests in faculty compensation above the Christian university market to attract and retain top academic talent. This includes named faculty chairs, research capacity funding, program director positions, and competitive salary ranges that exceed peer institutions. Breaking the Christian university faculty compensation ceiling is how the partner institution builds the academic product that earns the tuition premium.

**Academic program development.** New degree programs, expanded existing programs, online program scaling, specialized certifications, and interdisciplinary programs aligned with market demand. The MBA launched Fall 2025 and the BSN in development are the beginning, not the end, of academic program growth. KaNeXT supports the development and launch of programs that expand the partner institution's academic footprint within ABHE's accreditation scope and, over time, potentially through additional accreditation pathways.

**Student services excellence.** Dipson-powered academic advising, tutoring, career services, mental health and wellness, student life programming, and the high-touch student experience that HPU and similar premium institutions deliver. Dipson extends student support infrastructure without the per-student staffing cost that limits smaller institutions.

**Academic infrastructure.** Libraries, laboratories, research centers, technology, classrooms, academic buildings. The physical and digital academic environment that matches the tuition tier. Initial investment at Deerfield, substantially expanded at Miami Lakes as the campus comes online.

**Accreditation expansion pathway.** Supporting the partner institution's ABHE accreditation continuity and the 2028 reaffirmation cycle. Over time, supporting potential expansion of accreditation scope or pursuit of additional accreditations that support program growth beyond ABHE's current undergraduate and master's scope.

This investment is what earns the tuition premium. Athletic visibility draws attention to the institution. Academic quality retains and compounds enrollment once students experience the education firsthand. HPU spent $3B+ over 20 years building the academic product alongside the campus. The partner institution does not need 20 years because the platform, the intelligence, and the operator capacity accelerate the build.

A meaningful portion of the Core Operations budget in Year 1, and expanded allocations in Year 2 forward under Full IOA, is directed to institutional quality investment. Specific allocation is internal to KaNeXT's operating budget and is structured to support the institutional transformation that justifies the tuition trajectory.

---

## Year 1 Revenue Model: Athletics-Only Agreement

Year 1 operates under the Athletics-Only Agreement. KaNeXT captures athletic revenue and athlete-specific tuition share. The partner institution retains academic authority and all non-athletic revenue streams. Both parties receive direct partnership financial flows.

### KaNeXT Revenue (Year 1)

**60% of net athlete tuition.** Approximately 180-220 athletes enrolled across Varsity and development tiers. Mix of scholarship athletes (net tuition near zero) and development tier athletes (full tuition). Projected KaNeXT share of net athlete tuition: approximately $400,000 to $800,000.

**Athletic revenue (100% KaNeXT).** Game tickets, merchandise through fulfillment, sponsorships, D1 guarantee game fees, NIL marketplace processing fees, athletic broadcast revenue. Year 1 projection: $500,000 to $1.5M.

**Platform revenue from the partner institution scope.** KPay processing fees on partner institution commerce within athletic and KaNeXT-operated scope. Year 1 projection: $100,000 to $300,000.

**Housing operation.** Athletes pay room and board through financial aid, KPay, or family payment. Master-leased cluster housing with team operations. Year 1 housing operates approximately at breakeven to slight loss, stabilizing in Year 2.

**KaNeXT total operational revenue Year 1: approximately $1M to $2.5M.** This is modest against the $30M deployment because Year 1 is the launch investment, not the return year. The athletic engine is building visibility. Enrollment is compounding. The platform is generating data. Year 1 revenue is a launch data point, not the model return.

### Partner Institution Revenue (Year 1)

**Direct payments from KaNeXT.** Operational Partnership Fee of $500,000. Philanthropic Contribution of $500,000 to $1,000,000. Combined direct flow: $1,000,000 to $1,500,000.

**40% of net athlete tuition.** Approximately $270,000 to $530,000.

**New non-athlete enrollment tuition.** Athletic visibility drives approximately 300-600 net new residential enrollments plus online program growth. At partner institution current tuition of $7,740 per student, new non-athlete tuition revenue ranges from approximately $2.3M to $4.6M annually by end of Year 1.

**All existing partner institution revenue.** Academic tuition for existing non-athlete students, grant revenue, existing contributions, all baseline institutional revenue. No change to existing streams.

**Partner institution net new revenue Year 1 from the partnership: approximately $3.6M to $6.6M.** This is on top of the partner institution's current institutional baseline. SFBC's FY2024 total revenue was approximately $4.15M. The partnership in Year 1 alone creates new revenue approaching or exceeding SFBC's entire current institutional revenue base, without SFBC investing a dollar.

---

## Year 2 through Year 5 Revenue Model: Full IOA Expansion Path

Year 2 onward assumes Full IOA expansion activates per the criteria defined in the Athletics Operating Agreement. Under the Full IOA, KaNeXT operates institutional infrastructure across enrollment, platform deployment, campus operations (as Miami Lakes comes online), and institutional support, while the partner institution retains academic authority, curriculum governance, faculty, and ABHE accreditation.

Revenue allocation under Full IOA follows a negotiated split modeled substantially more favorably to the partner institution than the GCU-GCE historical precedent. The model below uses a 60/40 KaNeXT/partner institution tuition split on net tuition institution-wide. Room and board on KaNeXT-owned campus facilities flows primarily to KaNeXT as campus operator. Athletic revenue continues at 100% KaNeXT. Platform revenue flows continue to KaNeXT. Direct payments to the partner institution (Operational Fee and Philanthropic Contribution) continue at the Year 1 structure, scaling modestly with enrollment growth.

### Year 2 Revenue (Full IOA First Year)

Enrollment 5,600 to 6,400. Tuition average $7,000 to $8,000 blended across residential and online. Miami Lakes in construction, not yet operational. KaNeXT captures 60% of net tuition, all athletic revenue, all platform revenue. Room and board revenue still limited (Miami Lakes not open, the partner institution operates residential at Deerfield).

**KaNeXT Year 2 revenue: approximately $15M to $22M.** Partner institution Year 2 net new revenue: approximately $9M to $13M on top of baseline.

### Year 3 Revenue (Miami Lakes Phase 1 Opening)

Enrollment 10,300 to 11,700. Tuition average $9,000 to $10,500. Miami Lakes Phase 1 operational, initial students relocated. KaNeXT captures growing room and board revenue from Miami Lakes housing.

**KaNeXT Year 3 revenue: approximately $45M to $65M.** Partner institution Year 3 net new revenue: approximately $22M to $32M.

### Year 4 Revenue (Miami Lakes Majority Operations)

Enrollment 16,800 to 19,000. Tuition average $12,000 to $14,000. Miami Lakes housing majority of residential students. KaNeXT captures significant room and board, dining, and campus commerce revenue. D1 athletic transition pathway active.

**KaNeXT Year 4 revenue: approximately $155M to $220M.** Partner institution Year 4 net new revenue: approximately $70M to $100M.

### Year 5 Revenue (Christian HPU State)

Enrollment 24,600 to 27,700. Tuition average $15,000 to $18,000 blended across residential and online. Miami Lakes fully operational as the primary campus. Athletic programs approaching or achieving Division 1 membership. KaNeXT captures tuition share, room and board, dining, campus commerce, athletic revenue, platform revenue, and media revenue.

**KaNeXT Year 5 revenue: approximately $370M to $520M from the partner institution alone.** Year 5 net new revenue: approximately $160M to $220M.

---

## Revenue Streams Breakdown

The model captures revenue at three confidence tiers.

### Tier 1: Directly Modeled (High Confidence)

**Tuition share.** 60% KaNeXT, 40% SFBC on net athletic tuition Year 1, expanding to institution-wide under Full IOA Year 2+.

**Room revenue.** Athletic housing Year 1-3. Miami Lakes KaNeXT-owned housing Year 3+ scaling to majority Year 4+ and full Year 5.

**Board revenue.** Dining operations on KaNeXT-owned Miami Lakes campus Year 3+.

**Athletic revenue.** Tickets, sponsorships, D1 guarantee fees, merchandise, NIL marketplace fees. 100% KaNeXT.

**Operational Partnership Fee and Philanthropic Contribution.** Direct cash flow from KaNeXT to the partner institution, annually.

### Tier 2: Material Upside (Included in Upper Ranges)

**Platform processing fees.** KPay commerce at the partner institution scope, growing with enrollment.

**Campus commerce.** Parking, retail, events, conference hosting revenue on KaNeXT-owned Miami Lakes campus.

**Online program scale.** Expansion of online tuition revenue through aggressive marketing and program development.

**Athletic media revenue.** KTV broadcast monetization, conference media rights, D1 media rights pathway.

### Tier 3: Forward Optionality (Noted, Not Quantified)

**Intelligence licensing revenue.** Potential licensing of KaNeXT intelligence to other institutions as platform recognition grows. Documented in sister revenue model documents.

**Division 1 athletic revenue.** D1 membership unlocks NCAA media rights, tournament revenue, licensing, and the significant prestige premium attached to D1 status. Modeled as forward upside beginning Year 5 with full activation Year 6 or Year 7.

**Alumni and major donor revenue.** D1 alumni bases and prestige-tier Christian institutions attract donor capital at scale. Not modeled in the 5-year horizon but represents substantial forward upside.

**Mandate revenue.** KaNeXT governing body mandate revenue is documented in the mandate strategy document and sister revenue models. Not included in this partner institution-scoped institutional revenue model.

**Bank revenue.** Platform commerce deposit volume supports a future bank charter. Revenue from bank operations is documented separately. Not included here.

---

## Division 1 Forward Trajectory

Partner institution athletic programs operate at the NAIA Varsity level through Year 4. Division 1 transition planning activates in Year 4 once the Miami Lakes campus provides the infrastructure footprint D1 institutions require.

D1 membership requires NCAA application, compliance buildout, scholarship scale at NCAA minimums, facility requirements, and a multi-year transition period. Institutional resource commitments at D1 exceed NAIA by a substantial margin. KaNeXT's athletic infrastructure buildout at the partner institution is designed to scale into D1 requirements, not require rebuilding.

The strategic case for D1: institutional prestige rises materially at D1 status. Tuition pricing power rises. Enrollment inquiry rises. Donor capacity rises. NCAA media rights and tournament revenue become available. D1 is the athletic state that matches the Christian HPU institutional state. The partner institution at Miami Lakes with D1 athletic programs is operating in the same competitive category as Baylor, TCU, BYU, and other faith-based Division 1 institutions.

D1 transition is forward optionality. Revenue impact is noted as Tier 3 upside and not quantified in the five-year base case.

---

## Five-Year Summary

The combined picture across the five-year horizon:

### KaNeXT Cumulative Institutional Revenue (Partner institution scope only)

| Year | Revenue Range |
|---|---|
| Year 1 | $1M - $2.5M |
| Year 2 | $15M - $22M |
| Year 3 | $45M - $65M |
| Year 4 | $155M - $220M |
| Year 5 | $370M - $520M |
| **5-Year Cumulative** | **$586M - $829M** |

### Partner Institution Cumulative Net New Revenue (above current baseline)

| Year | Revenue Range |
|---|---|
| Year 1 | $3.6M - $6.6M |
| Year 2 | $9M - $13M |
| Year 3 | $22M - $32M |
| Year 4 | $70M - $100M |
| Year 5 | $160M - $220M |
| **5-Year Cumulative** | **$264M - $371M** |

SFBC's FY2024 total revenue was approximately $4.15M. The partnership's five-year cumulative net new revenue to the partner institution alone exceeds the institution's current annual revenue by more than sixty times in the base case and by nearly ninety times in the upper case. SFBC becomes a meaningful Christian university during the partnership horizon. The transformation is real, measurable, and compounding.

KaNeXT's five-year cumulative institutional revenue from the partnership alone substantially exceeds the Year 1 fund obligation of $45M at a 1.5x return to limited partners. The Year 1 raise is recoverable from the institutional revenue stream alone within the first three to four years. Platform revenue, mandate revenue, intelligence licensing, and bank revenue operate as additional return streams beyond this model's scope.

---

## Disclaimer

All revenue projections are forward-looking estimates based on current operational planning, market conditions, and assumptions about execution. Actual revenue depends on partnership execution, athletic performance, enrollment results, tuition pricing decisions, campus development timing, regulatory conditions, accreditation status, market conditions, and operational execution. Ranges reflect base case to upper case operational outcomes. Ranges are not minimum-to-maximum. Downside cases including partnership delay, enrollment shortfall, campus development delay, accreditation issues, or market disruption produce revenue below the ranges presented. No specific revenue figure, split allocation, campus timing, or Division 1 transition outcome is committed by this document. The Full IOA expansion path is conditional on Year 1 execution meeting the expansion criteria defined in the Athletics Operating Agreement. The Miami Lakes campus pathway is conditional on successful land acquisition from Graham Companies, which is not yet contracted. The Division 1 athletic transition is conditional on NCAA membership requirements, institutional readiness, and regulatory approval. Each material milestone in this model is a future condition requiring separate execution and separate evaluation at the time of decision.

<!-- FILE: 03_The_Money/revenue_model_platform_intelligence.md -->
<!-- ============================================================ -->

# KaNeXT Revenue Architecture: Platform and Intelligence

**KaNeXT LLC | Confidential**

---

## Purpose

This document presents the monetization layers of KaNeXT's platform and intelligence system. These streams are entirely excluded from the Institutional Revenue Model, which covers only the partner institution tuition, housing, dining, athletics, and operating partnership fees. Everything described here is additional upside beyond the institutional base case.

This model describes the architecture of how revenue is generated rather than assigning hard dollar projections to each line. Revenue from these streams depends on platform adoption, mandate execution, intelligence validation through competitive outcomes, and market timing. Where directional sizing is supportable, it is provided. Where it is not, the revenue category is described qualitatively.

---

## Intelligence Revenue

### What the Intelligence Does for One Sport

Basketball is the deepest example and the first sport fully validated. The following capabilities represent what the intelligence system produces for a single sport. Each is a potential revenue stream when commercial licensing opens in Year 3+ after the partner institution's held-in-house validation phase. During Year 1-3, mandate institutions receive the platform (OS, KTV, KPay, KVision, KStat) but not the deep evaluation intelligence; the partner institution retains exclusive access as the competitive moat.

**Recruiting intelligence.** Two-way matching between athletes and programs. Athletes see what level they should target and which schools need their specific trait profile. Coaches see which players in the national pool fit their system, ranked by system fit, KR, eligibility, and geography. Replaces recruiting databases (NCSA at $3,000-$6,000 per family) and scouting services.

**Transfer portal intelligence.** When a player enters the portal, the system immediately computes their system fit against every program on the platform. Coaches see which entrants improve their Team KR. Athletes see which programs need their profile.

**Scouting and game preparation.** Auto-generated opponent scouting reports: team overview, roster breakdown with KR and archetype for every player, offensive and defensive tendencies, matchup analysis, and game plan suggestions. Halftime and postgame coaching packets generated automatically from live StatKeeper data.

**Player development intelligence.** Trait-level development tracking over time. Development projections showing where a player can get to. Position-specific development plans identifying which skills to improve and by how much to reach the next competitive level.

**Team construction intelligence.** Roster optimization through system fit, archetype demand, and scholarship/NIL allocation. Simulation of roster scenarios: what happens to Team KR if this player is added, if that player transfers, if the system changes.

**Pro transition intelligence.** Entry KR and peak KR projections. Best-fit professional teams and leagues mapped by system. Specific trait improvements that would shift the projection. Development pathway from current level to professional viability.

**Pro front office intelligence.** Beyond draft projection, the intelligence system models the full operational landscape of professional sports: trade evaluation (what does each side gain or lose in KR and system fit), free agency analysis (which available players fill which team needs at what price), salary cap modeling (how does this move affect cap space over multiple years), CBA compliance (what is allowed under each league's collective bargaining agreement), and arbitration analysis (what is a player's value relative to comparable contracts). Each professional league operates under different rules (NBA soft cap with luxury tax, NFL hard cap, MLB no cap with luxury tax, soccer transfer windows and Financial Fair Play). The intelligence models each league's specific regulatory framework. This is a distinct product sold to professional front offices, agents, and media companies.

**Learning Lab.** Professional simulation environment: Film Room, War Room, Simulation Sandbox, Development Lab. Subscription-based access for coaches, scouts, front office professionals, and students.

**Certification.** Programs proving a coach, scout, or analyst can operate within the intelligence framework. Interactive assessment through KPlay.

**NIL valuation.** Player Transfer Value (PTV) connects player compensation to on-court value. Brand-to-athlete matching through intelligence. Compliance-governed deal execution through KPay.

**Data licensing.** Anonymized, aggregated game data licensed to media companies, fantasy platforms, betting analytics firms, and research organizations. The mandate produces 20,000+ games of structured data per year across competitive levels no existing provider covers.

### Multiply by 27+ Sports

Every capability listed above applies to every sport the intelligence system covers. The architecture is identical. The domain content changes (traits, positions, systems, legends), but the evaluation pipeline, simulation engine, system fit computation, and governance framework are structurally the same.

Currently specified: 27+ sport domains across the athletics intelligence architecture. The intelligence is currently expanding to additional sports and domains as the architecture proves portable across every team sport evaluated to date.

Some sports carry additional revenue mechanics. Soccer intelligence includes transfer fee modeling, loan system optimization, and sell-on clause valuation. Football intelligence covers 85-scholarship roster construction with position-specific draft projection. Each sport's intelligence produces the same core capabilities with sport-specific extensions.

### Smart Ball: Hardware-Enabled Intelligence Revenue

KaNeXT Smart Ball is a sensor-embedded equipment line covering 9 ball sports: basketball, baseball, football, soccer, softball, volleyball, tennis, rugby, and golf. Each ball is regulation-spec with an internal IMU (accelerometer and gyroscope), microcontroller, local storage, battery, and unique ball ID. The exterior feel is identical to a standard ball.

The ball captures execution truth that cameras cannot see: spin rate, spin axis, spin decay, release velocity, contact quality, rhythm consistency, and fatigue-driven mechanical degradation. Every touch, every pitch, every strike, every serve is timestamped and mechanically profiled independent of video.

The underlying architecture is the Unified Ball Truth Layer (UBTL), which standardizes raw sensor data across all 9 sports, and Sport Truth Adapters (STAs), which translate UBTL primitives into sport-specific execution events. A basketball STA detects shots, passes, and dribbles. A baseball STA detects pitches, hits, and throws. The architecture is the same. The sport-specific interpretation layer changes.

When paired with KVision video, Smart Ball data gains identity resolution (who executed the ball) and tactical context (game situation, defensive pressure). Ball-only data captures execution mechanics. Video captures context. Together they produce complete execution intelligence at a depth no existing analytics platform offers.

Smart Ball intelligence is monetized through tiered data access. Base-level data (event counts, timestamps, coarse execution indicators) is available at low cost. Advanced intelligence (pattern analysis, consistency tracking, execution quality scoring, fatigue modeling) is a premium subscription. The tiers expand data depth, not data access - teams never lose access to data they have already earned.

Revenue streams from Smart Ball:

- Intelligence subscriptions for practice and training data (per team, per sport, per season)
- Execution quality analytics sold to individual athletes and families
- Aggregate execution data licensed to equipment manufacturers, sports science researchers, and performance companies
- Integration with the existing KR evaluation pipeline (Smart Ball mechanical data becomes an input to trait scoring during live evaluation)
- KPlay simulation integration (real execution data feeds into game simulations)

Smart Ball extends the intelligence system from games (where cameras capture everything) into practices and training (where cameras are often absent but the majority of player development happens). This closes the last major gap in the evaluation pipeline: the system now has data on every competitive game AND every practice rep.

The hardware is not the revenue vector. The intelligence generated from the hardware is.

### Institutional Intelligence (Non-Sport)

The same architecture applies beyond athletics. The institutional intelligence architecture spans 27 modules organized into 5 operational domains: People and Talent (Workforce, Talent Acquisition, Student Success, Enrollment Management with RKR/PKR/PLKR/RCKR, Alumni, Admissions with T3-governed engagement scoring), Operations and Infrastructure (Technology, Real Estate, Operations, Facilities, Curriculum), Leadership and Governance (Strategic Planning, Risk and Legal, Compliance, Audit, Accreditation), Growth and External (Marketing, Grants, Fundraising, Communications, Acquisition with confidence degradation on suppression adjustments), and Finance and Assets (Treasury, Sales Revenue, Procurement, Financial, Endowment, Asset Management). These use the same KR framework, component scoring, system fit computation, and governance principles adapted to non-sport evaluation objects. The institutional intelligence architecture is production-complete and covers every operational dimension of a university, K-12 school, church, nonprofit, business, or sports organization.

Institutional intelligence is the longest-horizon revenue category. It depends on demonstrating that the architecture translates from sports (where it is validated through competitive outcomes) to institutional domains (where it is specified and in early validation). Revenue from institutional intelligence is not assumed in any near-term projection.

### Directional Sizing: Intelligence

The recruiting intelligence market alone (replacing NCSA, Hudl, and scouting services) represents a multi-hundred-million-dollar addressable market once commercial licensing opens in Year 3+. KaNeXT captures this market at institutional-IP tier pricing after the partner institution validates the architecture through visible competitive outcomes. Ancillary intelligence products (valuation lookups, recruiting match previews, scouting briefs) may generate modest Year 1-2 revenue at commodity pricing without compromising the core moat.

The Learning Lab and certification programs represent high-margin recurring revenue with low marginal cost per user. Data licensing grows automatically as mandate deployment increases structured game data volume. Professional scouting and draft intelligence is the highest per-customer value but the smallest initial customer base.

---

## OS and Platform Revenue

The platform generates revenue through the infrastructure layer regardless of which intelligence domain is active. Every institution on the platform, every user, and every transaction contributes to platform economics.

### Commerce Processing

5% flat processing on all transactions across mandate institutions, network schools, and all five modes. Tickets, merchandise, concessions, donations, tuition payments, event fees, and any commerce that flows through KPay.

When a fan pays by card, the card network takes approximately 3% underneath, leaving approximately 2% margin. When a fan pays from their KPay wallet, the transaction is closed-loop and the card network is eliminated. At current legacy processing rates, institutions pay 1.5-3.5% across multiple intermediaries. KPay internal settlement costs approximately 0.3%.

### Wallet and Banking Economics

The wallet generates revenue through multiple layers that compound with user adoption.

**Deposit float.** Every dollar sitting in a KPay wallet earns yield for KaNeXT. The bank earns the federal funds rate (or equivalent risk-free return) on aggregate deposits and pays users a lower APY. The spread is pure margin that scales linearly with wallet adoption. Float income grows with every mandate institution deployed, every wallet activated, every payroll direct deposit added.

**Remittance.** Targeting a fraction of the global average fee on international transfers. The US outbound remittance market is large and the cost gap between legacy providers and digital wallet-based transfers is substantial. Even modest capture of diaspora remittance activity represents significant recurring revenue at near-zero marginal cost per transaction.

**Interchange.** 1-2% on every KaNeXT Card transaction outside the ecosystem (Apple Pay, Google Pay, physical card). This monetizes every dollar a user spends outside the platform.

**Lending.** Upon charter acquisition: student loans, SBA-guaranteed loans, commercial lending, real estate lending, personal lending. KaNeXT has platform-native underwriting data (transaction history, income patterns, account tenure, behavioral signals) that traditional banks cannot access. Lower default risk enables better rates and wider margins.

**Treasury and institutional services.** Cash management, payroll processing, accounts payable for institutions on the platform. Each institution operating through KPay is a potential treasury client.

**CRA-motivated deposits.** Major banks seeking Community Reinvestment Act credit deposit funds into MDI-designated banks. KaNeXT Bank as an MDI receives these deposits at favorable terms, expanding the deposit base without marketing cost.

### Media and Advertising

KTV advertising across all institutions. KaNeXT owns the ad inventory. Pre-roll on replays, commercial insertions, sponsored pages, and targeted partner advertising. Premium subscriptions for advanced features. Podcast network advertising through the KaNeXT Sports Network.

### Merchandise and Fulfillment

Fulfillment center production margin across every storefront (mandate institutions, network schools, athlete NIL stores, brand stores across all modes, tournament events). Camera upgrade sales through the product ladder.

### Communications

VoIP company recurring revenue ($3.2-$4.4M ARR from existing client base at acquisition). Continues post-acquisition and offsets operating costs while the user base scales.

### OS and Intelligence Licensing

KaNeXT OS licensed to institutions outside the mandate and school network at $50,000-$500,000 per institution per year. Dipson licensed as a standalone institutional operations layer. KVision processing sold to D1 programs, professional leagues, and international federations.

### Gaming

Sports management simulation is a proven revenue category. The Football Manager franchise (Sports Interactive / Sega) has generated significant annual revenue from a single sport using generated players with no real intelligence architecture. It is among the most commercially successful simulation franchises in gaming.

KaNeXT builds the same category but with structural advantages Football Manager cannot replicate: real intelligence architecture powering the simulation (KR, KLVN, system fit, archetypes), multiple sports (Basketball GM, Football GM, Soccer GM, Baseball GM, and additional titles as the studio scales), real competitive levels and real league structures governing gameplay, and integration with the platform where real games, real stats, and real evaluations feed back into the simulation.

Revenue streams: game sales, in-game purchases (roster updates, content expansions, cosmetics), premium subscription tiers, KPlay course and content sales, business education tuition flowing through network schools (the simulation IS the curriculum for sports management degrees), and educational game subscriptions. The gaming business is high-margin, digitally distributed, and scales globally without requiring institutional deployment.

---

## Strategic Note

Intelligence revenue and platform revenue are not independent. The intelligence is what makes the platform valuable to institutions. The platform is what delivers the intelligence to users. Commerce processing exists because institutions adopted the OS. Institutions adopted the OS because the intelligence made their programs better. The separation in this document is for clarity. In practice, every revenue stream reinforces every other.

Intelligence revenue is not required for the institutional base case to reach operating profitability. It is additional upside that accelerates the path to profitability and increases the long-term enterprise value of the intelligence IP.

---

## Disclaimer

All revenue descriptions are forward-looking estimates. Platform and intelligence revenue streams are earlier-stage than institutional revenue and carry higher uncertainty. Actual results depend on product development, market adoption, pricing validation, mandate execution, competitive dynamics, and execution.


<!-- ============================================================ -->
<!-- FILE: 03_The_Money/revenue_model_multiplication.md -->
<!-- ============================================================ -->

# KaNeXT Go-to-Market Strategy: How the Platform Deploys and Compounds

**KaNeXT LLC | Confidential**

---

## Purpose

The Institutional Revenue Model presents the base case in hard numbers. The Platform and Intelligence Revenue Architecture presents what monetizes. This document explains how the system deploys and why it compounds. This is the go-to-market strategy and the revenue expansion logic in one document, because they are the same thing.

---

## Layer 1: The Institution

Everything starts with the institution. The partner institution is not just the first partner. It is the live laboratory where every product, every intelligence system, and every piece of the OS is deployed, tested, and proven in a real institutional environment with real students, real athletes, real faculty, and real operational conditions.

An institution is essentially a city. It has housing, dining, commerce, athletics, media, finance, communication, governance, enrollment, scheduling, transportation, and community life. Every system KaNeXT builds runs inside the partner institution first. The intelligence evaluates real athletes in real games. KPay processes real tuition, real concessions, real merchandise. KTV broadcasts real games to real audiences. Dipson handles real student questions, real coaching packets, real institutional communications. The VoIP system carries real phone calls. The wallet holds real money.

This is why KaNeXT acquires Miami Lakes and builds a campus at scale. The campus is the physical footprint for the full institutional operating system at maturity. Every building is wired for KaNeXT infrastructure. Every transaction flows through KPay. Every game is filmed by KVision cameras. Every student, athlete, faculty member, and visitor operates inside the OS. There is no legacy system to integrate with. The campus is built from the ground up on KaNeXT infrastructure.

The partner institution proves every claim in this data room. When the intelligence system says it can evaluate athletes, the proof is the partner institution's competitive results. When the OS says it can run an institution, the proof is the partner institution operating on it daily. When KPay says it can process institutional commerce, the proof is every transaction at the partner institution settling through the wallet. The institution provides the evidence for every claim in this data room before those claims are deployed elsewhere.

The partner institution's schedule is a deliberate GTM weapon. Six Varsity programs compete across multiple levels in Year 1 under USCAA/NCCAA/independent affiliation, with D1 guarantee games in basketball and selectively across other sports. This multi-sport, multi-level schedule is not incidental. It produces the highest-leverage outcome any small Christian college athletic program can produce: D1 wins across multiple sports from a sub-Division-I level, powered by an AI-driven intelligence system, at an institution most of the market has never heard of. Each such outcome creates national media exposure. A single D1 basketball upset generates SportsCenter coverage. Two or three D1 upsets across multiple sports in a single season generates a category story that reaches general-interest media. Every appearance drives app installs, KTV viewership, KPay transaction volume, and institutional inbound interest that compounds the user acquisition funnel without paid marketing spend. The partner institution is a theological school, which extends the reach further. Seminary networks, denominational leaders, pastor conferences, alumni groups, and adult ministry audiences layer a non-sports adult user base onto the athletic user base. Students, faculty, staff, alumni, parents, fans, seminary audiences, and adult ministry networks all operate inside the same app. One institutional deployment produces a diverse, multi-demographic user population that no sports-only or education-only platform captures.

---

## Layer 2: The Governing Body Mandate

The mandate is the go-to-market strategy. It is how the platform reaches scale without consumer marketing.

KaNeXT approaches the governing bodies that oversee collegiate athletics (NAIA, USCAA, NCCAA, GAAC, and additional state and international athletic associations). The offer: free cameras, free platform, free streaming for every school in the association. The only requirement is that every game is uploaded to the platform. The governing body gets a broadcast network, a statistics platform, and a media archive at zero cost. KaNeXT gets distribution.

The mandate functions as distribution infrastructure rather than a marketing channel. The governing body deploys the platform to every school in its association. KaNeXT does not need to sell to hundreds of schools one at a time. It partners with governing bodies and reaches their member schools through existing institutional relationships.

GAAC is the first mandate deployment. The relationship is strategic, not incidental. The partner institution is a charter GAAC member, and the principal funder of the partner institution is also the GAAC conference owner with active ambitions to expand GAAC into a scaled Christian college athletic league structured similarly to NCCAA, NAIA, and USCAA. KaNeXT's first mandate therefore operates inside a committed strategic partner relationship rather than a cold governing body pitch. GAAC member schools receive the full platform free. As the conference owner expands GAAC to additional small Christian colleges, each new member onboards to KaNeXT automatically. GAAC proves the mandate playbook, and the GAAC operator relationship accelerates time to NAIA, USCAA, and NCCAA deployment across the broader Christian college athletic ecosystem that is largely interconnected.

---

## Layer 3: The Platform Multiplies

Once people are on the platform through sports, they bring everything else with them.

**Organizations come on.** The parent who downloaded the app to watch a game is also a church member. The church joins the platform in Community mode. Now the church has a KTV channel, a KPay donation system, a merch store, a VoIP number, and Dipson handling member communication. The parent did not pitch the church on adopting enterprise software. They showed the pastor the app they already use.

**Businesses come on.** A local business sponsors the partner institution's athletic program. The sponsorship is processed through KPay. The business sees the platform and adopts Business mode. Now the business has a storefront, a payment system, a communication layer, and Dipson handling customer interactions.

**Families expand.** A diaspora family receives a free call from their child at a mandate institution through the VoIP layer. They download the app to receive the call. Now they have a wallet. They can send remittance at a fraction of legacy fees. They tell family members. Family members download the app. One free phone call creates a cluster of platform users across multiple countries without paid marketing.

**Students become lifetime users.** A student downloads the app at 14 to watch games. At 18, the wallet becomes their campus card (financial aid, tuition, dining, bookstore). At 22, they graduate with years of transaction history and route direct deposit to KPay. At 25, they use remittance, investing, every purchase generating interchange. At 30, their child starts playing sports. The cycle restarts. At every stage, every alternative feels like a downgrade.

Each person who enters through sports becomes a potential user of every platform capability: wallet, phone, media, commerce, intelligence, communication. Each organization they bring on adds another node of commerce volume, advertising inventory, and deposit activity. The platform does not grow by marketing to consumers. It grows by deploying to institutions and letting the institutional community expand organically.

---

## Depth Over Breadth at the Institutional Layer

The partner institution is the only institutional operating partner during the current planning horizon. This is intentional. The thesis is depth, not breadth.

KaNeXT does not spread capital and attention across multiple distressed institutional acquisitions in Year 1. KaNeXT deploys the full architecture at one institution and produces publicly attributable transformation: athletic championships, D1 upsets, enrollment growth, tuition premium, campus development, retention improvement, and institutional financial transformation.

The FOMO pipeline described in the institutional network strategy is the market response to visible transformation. Other institutions reach out as the partner institution matures through the partnership. These inbound institutions become potential future deployment opportunities after the partner institution is fully built out, not concurrent deployments dividing Year 1 attention. The multiplication at the institutional layer happens after the partner institution reaches the transformation markers that justify a second institutional deployment.

The platform multiplication described in Layer 3 operates at the user and organizational level, not the institutional operating partner level. Every platform user, every church, every business, every family, every mandate institution compounds the network. The institutional operating partner side stays disciplined at the partner institution.

---

## International Deployment

The mandate playbook deploys internationally through the same governing body structure. Identify the federation, deploy free cameras and platform, mandate upload. The architecture is sport-agnostic and language-agnostic.

Every international market that adopts multiplies commerce processing, advertising inventory, intelligence data, wallet adoption, remittance activity, and the user base for gaming, media, and merchandise. International deployment is excluded from current base-case projections and operates as upside to the projected financial outcomes.

---

## Sports Products

Owned tournament properties produced by KaNeXT, streamed through KTV and partner networks. Revenue from tickets, sponsorship, broadcasting rights, merchandise, and concessions. Every dollar settles through KPay. Showcase and camp events evaluated through the intelligence system. Guarantee game fees across basketball and football. Conference tournament hosting. These are detailed in the Tournament and Event Properties strategy doc.

---

## Intelligence Licensing

The intelligence product is held in-house during the partner institution's Year 1-3 validation phase as detailed in the Intelligence Value Creation Strategy doc. Mandate institutions receive the platform (OS, KTV, KPay, KVision, KStat) during this period but not the deep evaluation intelligence. Small-scale ancillary intelligence products (player valuation services, recruiting match previews, basic scouting briefs) may generate modest Year 1-2 revenue at commodity analytics-tool tier pricing without compromising the core moat. Full commercial intelligence licensing opens in Year 3+ at institutional-IP tier pricing after the partner institution has publicly demonstrated the architecture produces NAIA championships, D1 guarantee game upsets, pro placements, Olympic qualifiers, and institutional transformation markers. At that point, commercial revenue scales across coaching, professional, agent, media, data, institutional operator, and corporate market segments at pricing that reflects validated institutional IP rather than unproven analytics tooling.

---

## Revenue Resilience

The platform does not need every revenue stream to succeed. The institutional base case alone is designed to reach operating profitability on institutional revenue during the Year 4-5 horizon. Platform and intelligence revenue accelerate coverage and build long-term enterprise value.

No single revenue stream is a dependency. Commerce processing generates revenue the moment mandate institutions deploy, regardless of whether intelligence licensing, gaming, or international expansion have launched. Intelligence licensing revenue activates at institutional-IP tier in Year 3+ after the held-in-house validation phase, with ancillary intelligence products providing modest Year 1-2 revenue without compromising the core moat. The diversity of revenue architecture means any combination of streams succeeding produces meaningful economic value. The system is designed so that every stream reinforces every other.

---

## Disclaimer

All revenue descriptions are forward-looking estimates. The deployment and multiplication effects described depend on mandate execution, governing body partnerships, international deployment, platform adoption, and intelligence commercial market development. Actual results may differ materially from projections.


<!-- ============================================================ -->
<!-- FILE: 03_The_Money/revenue_model_os_platform.md -->
<!-- ============================================================ -->

# KaNeXT Revenue Model: OS Platform

**KaNeXT LLC | Confidential**

---

## Purpose

This document presents the platform revenue model for KaNeXT LLC. Platform revenue is generated by the infrastructure layers of the KaNeXT OS across all three institutional tiers: school network, governing body mandate, and the broader platform user base.

Platform revenue is excluded from the institutional base case (covered in the Institutional Revenue Model) and from intelligence licensing (covered in the Intelligence Revenue Model). This model captures the financial activity generated by the operating system itself: payments, commerce, media, communications, fulfillment, and institutional licensing.

All projections are directional estimates. Actual results depend on execution, user adoption, regulatory approvals, and market conditions.

---

## Revenue Streams

### 1. KPay Commerce Processing (5%)

Every transaction across every mandate institution and network institution flows through KPay's governed settlement chain. KaNeXT charges 5% flat processing on all commerce: tickets, merchandise, concessions, donations, event fees, and ancillary transactions.

At mandate institutions, this revenue is created from nothing. These schools had no digital ticketing, no online merch stores, and no governed commerce before KaNeXT. The 5% processing fee is on revenue that did not previously exist.

At network schools (IOA institutions), non-tuition commerce also flows through KPay. Athletic revenue, campus commerce, dining, parking, and events all settle through the governed chain.

**Drivers:** Number of institutions on the platform, average commerce volume per institution, wallet adoption rate (closed-loop wallet transactions eliminate card network fees and increase margin).

### 2. Wallet Float Income

Every KPay wallet balance is a deposit. The bank earns the spread between what it pays wallet users (target competitive APY, dependent on banking partner and charter status) and what it earns on aggregate deposits.

Deposit sources: student accounts, institutional operating accounts, remittance float, fund capital under management, philanthropic capital in institutional accounts, CRA-motivated corporate deposits from major banks seeking MDI credit.

**Drivers:** Total wallet users, average balance per user, deposit growth from mandate and network school adoption, spread between deposit yield and APY paid.

### 3. Remittance

International money transfer through KPay at competitive rates targeting a fraction of the global industry average (over 6%). The diaspora play through VoIP creates the international funnel. A student calls home for free. The parent downloads the app. The parent sends money home through KPay instead of Western Union.

**Drivers:** International user base, remittance volume per user, fee per transaction, corridor-specific pricing.

### 4. Interchange Income

Every KPay card transaction outside the KaNeXT ecosystem (Apple Pay, Google Pay, physical card) generates 1-2% interchange revenue for the bank. This scales with wallet adoption across all institutional layers.

**Drivers:** Card-active wallet users, average external spend per user.

### 5. KTV Advertising

Every person watching content on KTV is watching on a KaNeXT-owned media surface. KaNeXT controls the ad inventory. Ad opportunities: pre-roll on game replays, commercial insertions at natural breaks, sponsored team and school pages, in-app promotion, and partner advertising targeted by sport, region, level, and interest.

**Drivers:** Total viewing hours, ad fill rate, CPM rates, advertiser demand (growing with mandate footprint).

### 6. Bank Lending

The bank originates loans to borrowers already operating inside the KaNeXT ecosystem with underwriting data unavailable to traditional lenders. Lending categories: private student loans at network institutions, SBA-guaranteed loans to small businesses in the ecosystem, commercial lending to campus vendors and service providers, real estate lending for cluster housing near campus, and personal lending to platform users with established histories.

**Drivers:** Deposit base (determines lending capacity), loan demand, credit quality of borrowers, regulatory capital requirements.

### 7. Bank Treasury and Institutional Services

Network institutions require cash management, payroll processing, accounts payable, vendor payment systems, and financial reporting. The bank provides these as fee-based services.

**Drivers:** Number of network institutions, service fees per institution.

### 8. VoIP Recurring Revenue

The acquired VoIP company has an existing customer base producing $3.2M-$4.4M in annual recurring revenue. This revenue continues post-acquisition while KaNeXT scales the user base through mandate and network school deployment.

**Drivers:** Existing ARR, retention rate, new institutional deployments.

### 9. Merch Fulfillment Margin

The fulfillment center produces merchandise for every mandate institution, network institution, athlete NIL store, brand store across all modes, and KaNeXT-owned events. KaNeXT earns production margin on every unit plus 5% commerce processing on every transaction.

Illustrative unit economics: a $45 hoodie produces approximately $6 production margin plus $2.25 commerce processing. Tier 3 premium products carry higher margins.

**Drivers:** Total storefronts on the platform, average order volume per storefront, product mix (Tier 1/2/3), per-unit cost improvements at scale.

### 10. OS Licensing to Non-Mandate Institutions

KaNeXT OS licensed as a standalone institutional operating system to schools, K-12 systems, community and faith institutions, and businesses that are not mandate institutions and not under IOAs. These customers get the OS (identity, communication, financial settlement, media, scheduling, operations) without the intelligence layer or the athletic system.

**Pricing model:** Annual license per institution. Tiered by size and feature access. Estimated range: $10,000-$100,000 per institution per year depending on scale and mode.

**Addressable market:** Thousands of churches, private schools, community organizations, and small businesses seeking an integrated institutional operating system.

### 11. Camera Sales

Camera hardware sold to programs and institutions outside the mandate at retail pricing (Cam Sport $200, Sport+ $500, Broadcast $1,500, Pro $3,000+). KaNeXT earns hardware margin on every unit sold.

**Drivers:** Demand from non-mandate programs wanting KVision processing or KTV streaming capability.

---

## Revenue Projection (Directional)

| Stream | Y1 | Y2 | Y3 | Y4 | Y5 |
|---|---|---|---|---|---|
| KPay commerce (5%) | $2M | $8M | $20M | $40M | $75M |
| Wallet float | $0.5M | $2M | $5M | $12M | $25M |
| Remittance | $0.25M | $1M | $3M | $7M | $15M |
| Interchange | $0.25M | $1M | $3M | $6M | $12M |
| KTV advertising | $1M | $4M | $10M | $20M | $40M |
| Bank lending | $0M | $0.5M | $2M | $5M | $12M |
| Bank treasury services | $0M | $0.5M | $1M | $2M | $4M |
| VoIP recurring | $3.5M | $4M | $5M | $6M | $8M |
| Merch fulfillment | $0.5M | $2M | $5M | $10M | $20M |
| OS licensing | $0M | $0.5M | $2M | $5M | $12M |
| Camera sales | $0.5M | $1.5M | $3M | $5M | $8M |
| **Total platform revenue** | **$8.5M** | **$25M** | **$59M** | **$118M** | **$231M** |

These projections are directional and assume progressive adoption across the mandate footprint, institutional network expansion, wallet adoption, and bank charter approval. Year 1 is modest because Year 1 operates under the Partner Institution Athletics-Only Agreement with initial mandate footprint through GAAC charter membership (not the full governing body reach). The bank charter acquisition is a Year 2+ capital event. Years 3-5 reflect the compounding effect of institutional deployment across all three tiers as Full IOA activation, additional governing body partnerships, and the bank charter progressively expand the revenue base.

---

## Margin Profile

Platform revenue has a mixed margin profile. Commerce processing and advertising are high-margin (70-90%). Bank revenue depends on deposit cost, credit quality, and regulatory capital. VoIP is moderate-margin with existing infrastructure. Fulfillment has lower margins on Tier 1 products but improves with scale and Tier 3 mix. Camera sales carry hardware margin dependent on COGS management.

Blended estimated gross margin on platform revenue: 50-70%, improving as wallet adoption shifts transactions from card-based (higher cost) to closed-loop (lower cost).

---

## Strategic Notes

Platform revenue is excluded from the institutional base case. It represents additional upside beyond the institutional base case and accelerates the path to operating profitability.

The platform revenue model is driven by institutional deployment, not consumer marketing. Every mandate institution that deploys creates commerce volume, viewing hours, wallet adoption, and deposit growth without KaNeXT spending consumer acquisition dollars. The mandate is the distribution engine for platform revenue.

The bank charter is the inflection point for several streams (wallet float, lending, interchange, treasury services). Pre-charter, these streams operate through white-label partnerships at lower margins. Post-charter, margins expand as KaNeXT captures the full economics on owned rails.

---

## Disclaimer

All projections are forward-looking estimates. Actual results depend on institutional deployment pace, wallet adoption, bank charter timing, regulatory approvals, and market conditions. These projections do not constitute a guarantee of performance.
