<!-- FILE: 03_The_Money/operating_budget_expense_model.md -->
<!-- ============================================================ -->

# Operating Budget and Expense Model

**KaNeXT LLC | Confidential**

---

## Purpose

The Institutional Revenue Model presents gross revenue to KaNeXT across the Year 1 partnership. This document presents the cost side: what it costs to operate the partnership across all functions. This answers "what are the actual yearly operating costs?" with a structured estimate.

All figures are forward-looking estimates. Actual costs depend on hiring timelines, construction schedules, enrollment pace, vendor negotiations, and market conditions. A detailed line-item operating budget is maintained internally and updated quarterly.

---

## Cost Structure Overview

Under the Athletics Operating Agreement and subsequent Full IOA, operating costs are split between two entities:

**KaNeXT (Operating Partner)** funds and operates: technology infrastructure, athletic operations, enrollment and marketing, campus operations (housing, dining, facilities on KaNeXT-owned land from Year 3), media production, financial infrastructure, communications, and all non-academic institutional functions. Funded from KaNeXT's 60% tuition share under the Full IOA structure, 100% of non-tuition revenue, and fund capital during the ramp period.

**the partner institution (University)** funds from its 40% tuition allocation: faculty salaries and benefits, academic program development, accreditation compliance, academic advising, registrar and financial aid administration, library and academic resources, institutional governance, and board operations. The partner institution controls this allocation with sole discretion consistent with its ABHE accreditation obligations.

Year 1 operates under the Athletics-Only Agreement where KaNeXT funds athletic operations and platform deployment directly. Full IOA revenue splits activate Year 2 upon ABHE substantive change approval and DOE CIO determination.

---

## KaNeXT Operating Expenses by Category (Y1-Y5)

### 1. Academic Delivery (University-Side, Funded by 40% Allocation from Year 2)

These costs are not KaNeXT operating expenses. They are presented for completeness because an investor will ask about total institutional operating cost, not just KaNeXT's share.

| Line | Y1 | Y2 | Y3 | Y4 | Y5 |
|---|---|---|---|---|---|
| Faculty salaries and benefits | $3.0M | $6.5M | $13.0M | $22.0M | $32.0M |
| Academic administration | $0.8M | $1.5M | $2.5M | $4.0M | $5.5M |
| Accreditation and compliance | $0.3M | $0.5M | $0.7M | $1.0M | $1.2M |
| Academic resources and library | $0.2M | $0.4M | $0.8M | $1.2M | $1.5M |
| **University academic subtotal** | **$4.3M** | **$8.9M** | **$17.0M** | **$28.2M** | **$40.2M** |

Year 1 academic costs reflect the partner institution's existing operational scale plus modest expansion. Year 2 onward, the 40% tuition allocation grows with enrollment and tuition. As enrollment scales and sticker tuition rises, the academic allocation generates surplus that supports institutional reinvestment (new programs, faculty expansion, research, endowment contribution).

Online faculty costs (adjunct instruction) are included above. Online delivery infrastructure (LMS, enrollment, marketing, student support) is a KaNeXT operating cost below.

### 2. Athletic Operations (KaNeXT)

| Line | Y1 | Y2 | Y3 | Y4 | Y5 |
|---|---|---|---|---|---|
| Coaching salaries (scales with sport portfolio) | $3.5M | $6.0M | $10.5M | $13.0M | $16.0M |
| Scholarships and financial aid | $1.5M | $4.5M | $6.5M | $8.5M | $10.5M |
| NIL distribution | $0.8M | $2.5M | $3.5M | $4.5M | $5.5M |
| Travel and competition | $1.25M | $3.5M | $5.0M | $6.5M | $8.0M |
| Equipment and uniforms | $0.85M | $1.5M | $2.0M | $2.5M | $3.0M |
| Athletic training and medical | $0.7M | $1.2M | $1.7M | $2.2M | $2.7M |
| Game operations and facilities | $1.2M | $1.8M | $2.5M | $3.5M | $4.5M |
| Recruiting | $0.6M | $1.3M | $1.8M | $2.3M | $2.8M |
| Strategic reserve (mid-year hires, exceptions, Y2 activation prep) | $3.1M | $2.7M | $1.0M | $1.5M | $2.0M |
| **Athletic subtotal** | **$13.5M** | **$25.0M** | **$34.5M** | **$44.5M** | **$55.0M** |

Year 1 athletics operates under the Athletics-Only Agreement at the $13.5M allocation. The scholarship line is materially lower in Year 1 because NAIA Varsity scholarship equivalencies do not activate until Year 2 upon NAIA membership approval. Most Year 1 athletes pay tuition through Title IV federal aid plus standard institutional aid, receiving strategic scholarships only where recruiting requires. The Year 1 strategic reserve ($3.1M) is intentionally sized to cover mid-year hires as recruiting converts, scholarship exceptions, guarantee game opportunities, coaching transitions, and Year 2 NAIA activation planning (membership fees, expanded staffing, additional sport additions). Year 1 line items reconcile directly to the athletic_budget_by_sport buildup.

Year 2 onward, the 6 Varsity programs activate full NAIA scholarship equivalencies. The scholarship line reflects this step change. Coaching, travel, recruiting, and operational costs scale as the sport portfolio expands toward the Year 5 full athletic department target.

### 3. Enrollment and Marketing (KaNeXT)

| Line | Y1 | Y2 | Y3 | Y4 | Y5 |
|---|---|---|---|---|---|
| Enrollment engine operations | $1.5M | $3.0M | $5.0M | $7.0M | $9.0M |
| Digital marketing and advertising | $1.0M | $2.5M | $4.5M | $7.0M | $10.0M |
| Brand and content production | $0.5M | $1.2M | $2.0M | $2.5M | $3.0M |
| Admissions and student acquisition | $0.5M | $1.5M | $2.5M | $3.5M | $5.0M |
| **Enrollment subtotal** | **$3.5M** | **$8.2M** | **$14.0M** | **$20.0M** | **$27.0M** |

Year 1 enrollment operates at athletics scope (the enrollment engine supports athletic recruitment and the enrollment channels directly driven by athletic visibility). Year 2 onward, enrollment operates institution-wide under the Full IOA. Marketing spend scales with enrollment targets. Effective CAC depends on channel mix (athletic visibility, platform distribution network, and organic brand awareness lower the blended CAC significantly compared to pure digital acquisition).

### 4. Technology and Platform (KaNeXT)

| Line | Y1 | Y2 | Y3 | Y4 | Y5 |
|---|---|---|---|---|---|
| Engineering team (platform, intelligence, infrastructure) | $3.0M | $4.5M | $6.0M | $8.0M | $10.0M |
| Cloud infrastructure and hosting | $0.8M | $1.2M | $2.0M | $3.0M | $4.0M |
| AI and intelligence API costs | $0.5M | $1.0M | $1.5M | $2.0M | $3.0M |
| Camera and hardware deployment | $1.0M | $0.5M | $0.5M | $0.3M | $0.3M |
| Data and third-party services | $0.5M | $0.5M | $0.7M | $1.0M | $1.0M |
| **Technology subtotal** | **$5.8M** | **$7.7M** | **$10.7M** | **$14.3M** | **$18.3M** |

Technology costs are relatively lean because the intelligence architecture is built (50+ intelligence domains, 400+ files, 27+ sports - not an R&D expense), the platform is built on modern cloud infrastructure, and AI costs per interaction decrease as models become more efficient. Camera deployment is a Year 1 capital expense that declines rapidly.

### 5. Campus Operations (KaNeXT)

| Line | Y1 | Y2 | Y3 | Y4 | Y5 |
|---|---|---|---|---|---|
| Housing and dining operations | $2.0M | $4.0M | $9.0M | $18.0M | $25.0M |
| Facilities maintenance and grounds | $0.8M | $1.5M | $3.0M | $6.0M | $8.0M |
| Security and campus safety | $0.5M | $1.0M | $2.0M | $3.0M | $4.0M |
| Utilities | $0.5M | $1.0M | $1.8M | $3.5M | $5.0M |
| Transportation and parking | $0.2M | $0.3M | $0.6M | $1.0M | $1.5M |
| **Campus operations subtotal** | **$4.0M** | **$7.8M** | **$16.4M** | **$31.5M** | **$43.5M** |

Campus operations costs increase significantly in Year 4 when Miami Lakes becomes the majority campus. Year 1 costs reflect athletic housing via master lease and support for the partner institution's existing South Florida operations. Year 2 adds the Miami Lakes acquisition phase. Year 3 adds Miami Lakes Phase 1 operations. Year 4 and Year 5 reflect full Miami Lakes operations. Housing and dining is the largest campus operations expense and scales directly with on-campus enrollment. Housing and dining revenue substantially exceeds housing and dining operating cost at scale, generating significant margin.

### 6. Corporate and Administrative (KaNeXT)

| Line | Y1 | Y2 | Y3 | Y4 | Y5 |
|---|---|---|---|---|---|
| Executive and management team | $1.5M | $2.2M | $2.8M | $3.3M | $3.8M |

| Office and administrative | $0.4M | $0.5M | $0.6M | $0.8M | $1.0M |
| **Corporate subtotal** | **$4.0M** | **$5.6M** | **$7.4M** | **$9.5M** | **$11.7M** |

### 7. Online Program Delivery (KaNeXT)

| Line | Y1 | Y2 | Y3 | Y4 | Y5 |
|---|---|---|---|---|---|
| LMS and delivery infrastructure | $0.3M | $0.6M | $0.9M | $1.3M | $1.8M |
| Online student support and advising | $0.3M | $0.8M | $1.8M | $3.5M | $5.5M |
| Online marketing (incremental to enrollment above) | $0.2M | $0.8M | $1.8M | $2.8M | $3.8M |
| **Online program subtotal** | **$0.8M** | **$2.2M** | **$4.5M** | **$7.6M** | **$11.1M** |

Online delivery costs scale with online enrollment. The partner institution's existing Populi infrastructure and online operations base accelerates Year 1 deployment. Adjunct faculty costs for online courses are included in the University's academic allocation above.

---

## Total Operating Expense Summary

| Category | Y1 | Y2 | Y3 | Y4 | Y5 |
|---|---|---|---|---|---|
| Athletic operations | $13.5M | $25.0M | $34.5M | $44.5M | $55.0M |
| Enrollment and marketing | $3.5M | $8.2M | $14.0M | $20.0M | $27.0M |
| Technology and platform | $5.8M | $7.7M | $10.7M | $14.3M | $18.3M |
| Campus operations | $4.0M | $7.8M | $16.4M | $31.5M | $43.5M |
| Corporate and administrative | $4.0M | $5.6M | $7.4M | $9.5M | $11.7M |
| Online program delivery | $0.8M | $2.2M | $4.5M | $7.6M | $11.1M |
| **Total KaNeXT operating expense** | **$31.6M** | **$56.5M** | **$87.5M** | **$127.4M** | **$166.6M** |
| University academic expense (40% allocation from Y2) | $4.3M | $8.9M | $17.0M | $28.2M | $40.2M |
| **Total institutional operating expense** | **$35.9M** | **$65.4M** | **$104.5M** | **$155.6M** | **$206.8M** |

---

## Operating Margin Analysis

These margins reflect the partner institution institutional revenue only. The revenue in this table is exclusively from the Institutional Revenue Model: tuition, housing, dining, athletics, and operating partnership fees. It does not include any revenue from the Platform and Intelligence Revenue Architecture: commerce processing across the distribution network, wallet float, remittance, advertising, intelligence licensing, data licensing, OS licensing, bank revenue, or any other platform revenue stream.

All platform and intelligence revenue is additional income on top of these margins. Most platform revenue streams operate at significantly higher margins than institutional operations (software and intelligence licensing at 80-90%+, commerce processing at 60%+, wallet float at near-100%, data licensing at 90%+). The actual total KaNeXT margin at scale, including platform and intelligence revenue, would be materially higher than what this institutional-only analysis shows.

This is intentional. The institutional base case is presented in isolation to demonstrate that the partner institution operations alone generate strong and growing margins without relying on any platform or intelligence revenue to be profitable. Everything from the platform and intelligence architecture is upside.

| Metric | Y1 | Y2 | Y3 | Y4 | Y5 |
|---|---|---|---|---|---|
| KaNeXT gross revenue (institutional only) | $1M-$2.5M | $15M-$22M | $45M-$65M | $155M-$220M | $370M-$520M |
| KaNeXT operating expense | $31.6M | $56.5M | $87.5M | $127.4M | $166.6M |
| **KaNeXT operating income (institutional only)** | **($29M) to ($30M)** | **($35M) to ($42M)** | **($23M) to ($42M)** | **$28M to $93M** | **$203M to $353M** |
| **Operating margin (institutional only)** | **negative** | **negative** | **negative to modest** | **18% to 42%** | **55% to 68%** |

Year 1 through Year 3 operate at institutional operating losses funded by the $33M capital raise plus platform and intelligence revenue streams running in parallel. Year 4 is the crossover year where institutional operations reach operating profitability on the base case. Year 5 institutional margins are strong and compound with platform and intelligence revenue operating at higher margins in parallel.

Operating margin improves materially from Year 1 to Year 5 because revenue scales faster than costs. This is the projected operating profile if enrollment, tuition, and athletic revenue assumptions perform as modeled. Actual margins depend on the pace and magnitude of enrollment growth, housing occupancy from Miami Lakes, athletic revenue maturation, online program scaling, and Full IOA activation timing. The margins shown here are achievable under the assumptions but should not be read as guaranteed outcomes.

---

## Enrollment Sensitivity

The operating model is sensitive to enrollment pace. The following illustrates the impact of a 25% enrollment shortfall across all years:

| Metric | Y1 Base | Y1 Downside | Y3 Base | Y3 Downside |
|---|---|---|---|---|
| Total enrollment | 2,100 | 1,575 | 11,000 | 8,250 |
| KaNeXT revenue (midpoint) | $1.8M | ~$1.3M | $55M | ~$42M |
| KaNeXT operating expense | $31.6M | ~$28M | $87.5M | ~$77M |
| Operating outcome | ($29.8M) | ($26.7M) | ($32.5M) | ($35M) |

Costs do not decrease proportionally with enrollment because athletics, technology, campus infrastructure, and corporate expenses are largely fixed or semi-fixed. Enrollment and marketing costs flex partially. Housing and dining costs flex with occupancy. Faculty costs (University-side) flex with course sections. The downside scenario illustrates that the institutional operating loss in early years widens under enrollment shortfall. Capital raised is sized to support operating losses through Year 3 on the base case; a sustained 25% shortfall extends the time to operating profitability and may require bridge capital for Year 4.

Platform and intelligence revenue streams operating in parallel provide partial offset against institutional operating losses and are not included in this sensitivity table.

---

## Fixed vs Variable Cost Structure

Understanding which costs flex with enrollment and which do not is critical to evaluating operating risk.

**Largely fixed (do not decrease significantly with lower enrollment):** Athletic operations (coaching staff, scholarships, travel, equipment are committed before enrollment is known). Technology and platform (engineering team, cloud infrastructure, AI costs). Corporate and administrative (executive team, legal, finance, insurance). These represent approximately 70% of Year 1 KaNeXT operating expense.

**Semi-variable (flex partially with enrollment):** Enrollment and marketing (spending can be reduced but at the cost of future growth). Campus operations (some facilities costs are fixed, but housing/dining staffing and food costs flex with occupancy). Online program delivery (adjunct faculty and support scale with online enrollment).

**Variable (flex directly with enrollment):** Faculty costs on the University side scale with course sections needed. Housing and dining food and staffing costs scale with occupancy.

---

## Year 1 Cash Flow Bridge

Year 1 KaNeXT institutional revenue ($1M-$2.5M) is materially below Year 1 operating expense ($31.6M). The Year 1 operating gap is funded from the $33M capital raise ($30M fund plus $3M SAFE). Year 1 capital deployment prioritizes athletic operations ($13.5M), platform infrastructure, enrollment engine launch, and institutional support payments to the partner institution.

Years 2-3 continue to require fund capital for operating losses as revenue ramps. Year 4 is the crossover year where institutional operating income moves positive on the base case. Year 5 institutional operations generate substantial positive operating income.

Platform and intelligence revenue streams running in parallel provide additional cash flow not included in this institutional-only analysis. The full KaNeXT revenue picture (institutional plus platform plus intelligence) provides materially stronger cash flow support than the institutional-only view.

---

## What This Model Does Not Include

This is the partner institution institutional operating budget only. It does not include: operating costs for mandate deployment beyond the initial GAAC rollout (covered in the Mandate Economics doc), platform and intelligence product costs beyond the partner institution institutional use, revenue and costs from intelligence licensing in coaching, pro, agent, media, and corporate markets (covered in the Intelligence Value Creation Strategy doc), or bank operating expenses post-charter. Each of these is addressed in its respective document.

---

## Key Assumptions and Sensitivities

**Faculty costs** assume a student-to-faculty ratio consistent with ABHE standards and premium Christian higher education benchmarks. Average loaded faculty cost rises as the partner institution invests in faculty compensation above the Christian university market per the institutional quality investment thesis.

**Coaching salaries** assume competitive D1 mid-major compensation with intelligence-system and NIL advantages contributing to the competitive recruiting edge. Head coach salaries range across sport and level. The total coaching compensation line grows with sport portfolio expansion from Year 1 6 sports toward Year 5 full athletic department.

**Housing and dining** margins are significant because KaNeXT owns the Miami Lakes facilities and operates them directly rather than outsourcing to a third-party management company. The $1/year the partner institution ground lease structure means KaNeXT captures housing, dining, and campus commerce revenue while the partner institution captures tuition share.

**Technology costs** are lean relative to revenue because the intelligence architecture is specified IP, reducing (but not eliminating) R&D uncertainty. Ongoing platform engineering, maintenance, security, integration, and infrastructure scaling are real costs that grow with institutional complexity and user base. The estimates above account for this growth.

**The largest cost sensitivity is enrollment pace.** If enrollment grows slower than projected, revenue declines faster than costs can be reduced. The fixed-cost structure described above means margin compression is the primary risk in a slower-enrollment scenario. The intelligence architecture (PLKR, RKR, PKR, RCKR described in the enrollment funnel model) is specifically designed to detect pace deterioration early and trigger intervention.

---

## Disclaimer

All cost estimates are forward-looking projections. Actual operating costs depend on hiring timelines, vendor negotiations, construction schedules, enrollment pace, regulatory requirements, and market conditions. These estimates do not constitute a guarantee of performance. The Year 1 operating loss profile is funded by the $33M capital raise; sustained enrollment underperformance or partnership execution delays may require bridge capital or operating expense reductions beyond what this document models.


<!-- ============================================================ -->
<!-- FILE: 03_The_Money/athletic_budget_by_sport.md -->
<!-- ============================================================ -->

# Athletic Budget by Sport and Level

**KaNeXT LLC | Confidential**

---

## Purpose

The Operating Budget shows athletic operations scaling from Year 1 through Year 5. This document breaks the Year 1 athletic budget down by sport, by level, and by expense category. It is the backup doc behind the athletic expense line in the Capital Deployment and Operating Budget.

Year 1 operates under the Partner Institution Athletics-Only Agreement. Six Varsity sports × two teams (Varsity + Development) = 12 teams across approximately 180-220 athletes. Year 1 is not yet NAIA: the NAIA application window (April 15, 2026) has passed. The next application window is October 15, 2026, with decision in April 2027 and active NAIA membership beginning Fall 2027 (Year 2). Year 1 competitive affiliation is USCAA, NCCAA, Independent, or a combination at the Varsity level, plus GAAC for Development. Year 1 scholarships are strategic (minimal scholarships deployed only where recruiting requires, with reserve for exceptional situations), not NAIA-equivalency caps.

Year 2 activates NAIA membership and scholarship equivalencies. Years 2-5 scale additional sports, full multi-level programs, and full-equivalency scholarship budgets.

---

## Coaching Staff Model

KaNeXT pays above NAIA market rates for Varsity head coaches and key assistants. Year 1 focuses on building the Varsity coaching staff at premium salaries with Development coaches supporting the second team in each sport. Cross-sharing across related programs keeps the staff count efficient in Year 1.

### Salary Ranges by Role

| Role | Salary Range | Notes |
|---|---|---|
| Head Coach (revenue sport: basketball) | $100,000-$150,000 | Above NAIA median ($50-75K). Justified by program scale and national visibility. |
| Head Coach (Olympic sport) | $60,000-$90,000 | Above NAIA median ($35-50K). Full-time position, not split with teaching duties. |
| Assistant Coach (revenue sport) | $50,000-$75,000 | Above NAIA median. Full-time, no teaching requirement. |
| Assistant Coach (Olympic sport) | $35,000-$55,000 | Full-time coaching positions. |
| Development Team Head Coach | $40,000-$55,000 | Development squad head coaches. Often promoted from assistant role. |
| Graduate Assistant / Intern | $15,000-$25,000 | Stipend plus tuition. Development pipeline for coaching staff. |
| Athletic Trainer (shared across sports) | $50,000-$65,000 | Certified athletic trainers. 2-3 needed Y1, growing in subsequent years. |
| Strength and Conditioning (shared) | $55,000-$70,000 | 1-2 staff Y1, growing in subsequent years. |

### Year 1 Staffing: 6 Varsity Sports with Development Second Teams

Year 1 deploys six Varsity sports: Men's Basketball, Women's Basketball, Men's Soccer, Women's Soccer, Men's Volleyball, Women's Volleyball. Each sport fields a Varsity team and a Development team (12 teams total). The exact sport portfolio is calibrated to maximize competitive quality and Title IX alignment within the Y1 capital envelope and the athlete pool the recruiting engine delivers.

| Sport | Varsity Staff | Dev Staff | Shared | Est. Salary Cost |
|---|---|---|---|---|
| Men's Basketball | 1 HC + 2 Asst + 1 GA | 1 HC (promoted from Asst) | - | $340K |
| Women's Basketball | 1 HC + 1 Asst + 1 GA | 1 HC | - | $260K |
| Men's Soccer | 1 HC + 1 Asst | 1 HC | - | $195K |
| Women's Soccer | 1 HC + 1 Asst | 1 HC | - | $195K |
| Men's Volleyball | 1 HC + 1 Asst | 1 HC | - | $165K |
| Women's Volleyball | 1 HC + 1 Asst | 1 HC | - | $165K |
| Shared staff (trainers, S&C, admin) | - | - | 4-5 | $275K |
| **Total Y1 coaching/staff** | | | **~24-25** | **~$1.6M** |

**Total Y1 coaching and staff with benefits (25-30%): approximately $2.1M.**

The balance of the $3.5M Y1 coaching and staff line (approximately $1.4M) provides budget for mid-year hires as recruiting converts, performance bonuses, coaching recruitment costs, relocation, and reserve for Year 2 expansion planning (additional sports, NAIA-equivalency staffing, and dedicated assistants across all Varsity programs).

### Phased Staff Growth (Y2-Y5)

**Year 2 (NAIA activation):** Add dedicated assistants across all Varsity sports. Expand shared support staff. Begin planning additional Varsity sports (e.g., cross country, track, baseball/softball) for Year 3. Total staff grows to approximately 35-40.

**Year 3:** Full assistant coaching staff at Varsity level across all sports. Multi-level programs (Varsity + Development + potentially JV) in several sports. Expanded sport portfolio (~10-12 Varsity sports). Total staff grows to approximately 50-55.

**Year 4-5:** Full multi-level deployment. Every sport has Varsity plus at least one development team with dedicated coaching. Full Varsity sport portfolio (~16 sports covering all NAIA-sponsored opportunities). Total staff reaches 65-75 by Y5. Coaching line reaches approximately $16-20M in Y5 at full deployment.

---

## Scholarship Model (Year 1)

Year 1 scholarships are strategic, not NAIA-equivalency caps (NAIA membership is Year 2). Most athletes receive Title IV federal financial aid (Pell Grants and federal student loans) plus standard the partner institution institutional aid. Strategic scholarships deploy only where recruiting requires it, with reserve for exceptional situations.

| Source | Estimated Y1 Cost |
|---|---|
| Strategic scholarships (recruiting-driven, exceptional situations) | ~$1.2M-$1.8M |
| Title IV + institutional aid (standard, not athletic-specific) | n/a (standard financial aid, not athletic budget) |
| **Y1 athletic scholarship total** | **~$1.2M-$1.8M** |

Year 1 total athlete count across 12 teams: approximately 180-220 athletes (approximately 12-18 per team depending on sport). The strategic approach preserves capital for coaching compensation, recruiting infrastructure, NIL reserves, and operational quality while the athletic engine proves out. Savings from strategic scholarship allocation deploy to premium coaching compensation and facilities quality.

### Year 2+ NAIA Equivalency Activation

Upon NAIA membership approval (expected April 2027 for Fall 2027 activation), scholarship structure shifts to NAIA equivalency model. Year 2 scholarship budget scales to approximately $3-5M across the Varsity sport portfolio. Year 5 at full deployment approaches $8-10M across a full NAIA sport portfolio.

NAIA academic exemptions (high-GPA students do not count against scholarship limits) reduce effective scholarship cost per equivalency. The net effect is that NAIA equivalency budgets represent gross maximum; actual net cost depends on academic profile of recruited athletes.

---

## Travel and Competition (Year 1)

Year 1 travel includes full Varsity schedules across the six sports, Development team competition, and D1 guarantee games. The intelligence system (Dipson) selects D1 opponents where the partner institution projects competitive based on Team KR analysis.

| Sport | Est. Travel Cost | Notes |
|---|---|---|
| Men's Basketball | $300K | Entire non-conference D1 guarantee schedule (12-16 games), ESPN+ broadcasts. Guarantee travel typically covered by host. |
| Women's Basketball | $200K | Select D1 guarantee games plus Varsity schedule. |
| Men's Soccer | $150K | Regional competitive schedule. |
| Women's Soccer | $150K | Regional competitive schedule. |
| Men's Volleyball | $100K | Conference/regional schedule. |
| Women's Volleyball | $100K | Conference/regional schedule. |
| Development travel across all sports | $250K | Regional competition, bus travel, shorter schedules. |
| **Total** | **~$1.25M** | |

D1 guarantee game travel is typically funded by the host institution (the D1 school pays a guarantee fee plus travel expenses). Net travel cost for guarantee games is minimal beyond lodging extensions. The $1.25M estimate covers Varsity conference/non-conference, D1 guarantees where not host-funded, and Development schedules.

---

## Equipment and Uniforms (Year 1)

| Category | Est. Cost | Notes |
|---|---|---|
| Basketball (M+W, Varsity + Dev) | $180K | Uniforms, shoes, practice gear, basketballs. Revenue sport receives premium outfitting. |
| Soccer (M+W, Varsity + Dev) | $100K | Uniforms, balls, goals, training equipment. |
| Volleyball (M+W, Varsity + Dev) | $100K | Uniforms, nets, balls, training equipment. |
| Shared equipment (weight room, training) | $400K | Year 1 capital expense for weight room buildout, training facility equipment. Declines in subsequent years. |
| Laundry and maintenance | $60K | |
| **Total** | **~$840K** | Declines to ~$500K-$700K in subsequent years as capital items are purchased. |

---

## Other Athletic Expenses (Year 1)

| Category | Est. Cost | Notes |
|---|---|---|
| NIL distribution | $0.8M | Governed through KPay. Allocated by PTV intelligence. Y1 scaled appropriately to team count and program stage. |
| Athletic training and medical | $0.7M | Certified trainers, team physicians (contracted), insurance, rehabilitation. |
| Game operations and facilities | $1.2M | Officials, game-day staff, commercial facility partnerships (gym and practice venues in the South Florida market), event production. |
| Recruiting | $0.6M | Campus visits, recruiting travel, prospect hosting, evaluation events. Intelligence system reduces recruiting cost by identifying high-fit targets more efficiently than traditional scouting travel. |
| Media production (KTV athletic) | Included in platform budget | KTV infrastructure is centralized across the platform, not double-counted here. |
| **Total** | **~$3.3M** | |

---

## Year 1 Athletic Budget Summary

| Category | Y1 Estimate |
|---|---|
| Coaching salaries and staff (including benefits) | $3.5M |
| Strategic scholarships | $1.5M |
| NIL distribution | $0.8M |
| Travel and competition | $1.25M |
| Equipment and uniforms | $840K |
| Athletic training and medical | $0.7M |
| Game operations and facilities | $1.2M |
| Recruiting | $0.6M |
| Reserve and contingency | $3.1M |
| **Total Y1 athletic budget** | **$13.5M** |

This reconciles to the $13.5M Athletics line in the Capital Deployment breakdown. The reserve/contingency covers mid-year hires, scholarship exceptions, guarantee game opportunities, coaching transitions, and Year 2 activation planning (NAIA membership fees, expanded staffing, additional sport additions).

---

## Year 2-5 Growth Assumptions

Athletic costs scale along the following drivers.

**Coaching salaries** grow significantly in Y2 as NAIA activates and additional assistants are added across all Varsity sports. Y2 coaching: approximately $5.5-6.5M. Y5 estimate at full multi-level deployment across ~16 Varsity sports: approximately $16-20M.

**Scholarships** transition from strategic ($1.5M Y1) to NAIA-equivalency model (Y2+). Y2 scholarships: approximately $3-5M. Y5 at full portfolio: approximately $8-10M. Scholarship cost also grows with COA increases (Y1 the partner institution tuition ~$7,740 rising toward approximately $20,000-$23,500 by Y5 per the Institutional Revenue Model).

**NIL** grows from $0.8M to $4-6M by Y5 as athletic revenue expands and the NIL marketplace matures. NIL allocation is guided by PTV intelligence and funded from athletic revenue as the program grows.

**Travel** grows with expanded sport portfolio, longer-distance competition, conference scheduling (NAIA competition from Y2), and additional Varsity programs. Y5 estimate: $5-7M.

**Equipment** declines from Y1 (capital buildout year) then stabilizes at $700K-$1.0M annually for replacements and new sport additions, rising to $1.5-2M by Y5 with full sport portfolio.

**Game operations** grows significantly in Y4-Y5 with the opening of the Miami Lakes campus (Y4 target) and purpose-built athletic facilities. Y5 estimate: $3-5M.

**Recruiting** grows modestly as the intelligence system makes recruiting more efficient over time. The system identifies high-fit targets from the 250,000+ athlete database, reducing the need for expensive in-person scouting travel.

---

## Athletic Revenue vs Athletic Cost

Year 1 athletic operations are fully funded by the $13.5M athletics allocation from the Year 1 $33M partnership raise. Athletic revenue (ticketing, guarantee game fees, media, sponsorship) ramps from modest in Year 1 to material by Year 3. The athletic operating budget reaches scale through Year 5 alongside enrollment-driven institutional revenue under the Full IOA.

Athletic revenue projection and athletic operating budget projections are documented in the Institutional Revenue Model and Operating Budget Expense Model. The athletic system operates at an operating deficit in early years, by design: Year 1-3 is the investment period during which the athletic engine is built, the brand is established, and the media/content/data flywheel begins generating the returns described throughout the data room. The deficit shrinks each year as athletic revenue scales, and crossover to athletic operating breakeven occurs between Year 4 and Year 5 per the Operating Budget Expense Model.

The athletic deficit in early years is not a failure. It is the investment period during which the athletic engine is built and the institutional partnership matures.

---

## Disclaimer

All athletic budget figures are forward-looking estimates. Actual costs depend on hiring outcomes, scholarship awards, travel scheduling, facility availability, equipment procurement, NAIA membership timing, and market conditions. Coaching salary ranges reflect above-market NAIA compensation intended to attract strong talent; actual salaries will be determined through individual negotiations. Year 1 sport portfolio may be adjusted within the six-Varsity-sport envelope based on recruiting conversion, competitive affiliation availability (USCAA, NCCAA, Independent), and Title IX alignment.

# Enrollment Funnel and Conversion Model

**KaNeXT LLC | Confidential**

---

## Purpose

The Institutional Revenue Model projects enrollment growing from approximately 2,100 in Year 1 to 24,600-27,700 by Year 5. This document breaks down how those students are acquired: through which channels, at what conversion rates, at what cost, and under what governed intelligence. This is the operational detail behind the enrollment assumptions that drive the institutional revenue projections.

The model is not traditional enrollment marketing. The model is governed by the KaNeXT Enrollment Management Intelligence architecture, which operates across the full student lifecycle: acquisition, retention, persistence, and recovery. Enrollment forecasting, channel optimization, and retention outcomes are all tracked through the intelligence system. Every projection in this document is governed by the same architecture.

All figures are modeled estimates. Actual conversion rates, CAC, and channel mix depend on execution, market response, brand development, and competitive dynamics.

---

## Enrollment by Source

partner institution enrollment comes from four distinct channels. Each has different economics, different conversion profiles, and different scaling characteristics.

### Channel 1: Athletic Recruiting (On-Campus)

Athletes recruited through the intelligence system and the national player pool. This is the enrollment anchor in Year 1 and grows substantially as multi-level teams deploy across expanding sport coverage.

| Metric | Y1 | Y3 | Y5 |
|---|---|---|---|
| Varsity athletes | 150 | 350 | 500 |
| Development tier athletes | 70 | 200 | 400 |
| **Total athletic enrollment** | **220** | **550** | **900** |

The multi-level athletic structure is a major enrollment driver. Every Varsity team, every development tier team, and every additional sport added is filled with enrolled students paying tuition. The partner institution launches with six Varsity sports in Year 1 and scales to 14 sports by Year 5, each with Varsity plus at least one development tier. The athletic system alone contributes approximately 900 enrolled students by Year 5, generating continuous content for KTV, continuous visibility for the institutional brand, and continuous data for the intelligence system.

Conversion model: the intelligence system identifies athletes across the national player pool of 250,000+ evaluated athletes across every college level. Coaches use system fit, KR, and academic eligibility filters to build targeted prospect lists. Recruiting communication happens through the governed platform. Conversion from prospect to enrolled student depends on scholarship offer, NIL package, institutional fit, and competitive alternatives.

Estimated Year 1 funnel: 4,000 prospects identified by intelligence > 1,500 contacted > 400 campus visits or virtual engagements > 250 offers extended > 160 commitments (Varsity recruiting class). Development tier athletes add 60 enrollments for total athletic enrollment of approximately 220 in Year 1. Yield on offers (commitments divided by offers) is estimated at 60-70%, which is high relative to typical NAIA programs because of the scholarship, NIL, coaching, and intelligence advantages.

CAC for athletic recruits is low in dollar terms because the intelligence system replaces expensive scouting travel and recruiting services. The cost is embedded in coaching staff salaries and platform operations rather than per-student marketing spend.

### Channel 2: Athletic Visibility and Brand (On-Campus)

Non-athlete students attracted by the institutional transformation, athletic visibility, national media coverage, and Christian mission. Sports is consistently one of the strongest drivers of institutional visibility and application interest at every level of higher education. At the partner institution, the athletic visibility strategy is designed to produce outsized awareness relative to institutional size.

| Metric | Y1 | Y3 | Y5 |
|---|---|---|---|
| Brand-driven on-campus students | 400 | 2,800 | 5,000 |
| Retained existing the partner institution students | 900 | 700 | 500 |

The visibility engine: the partner institution's Varsity men's basketball team plays 12-16 Division 1 guarantee games per year, broadcast on ESPN+ through host D1 school media agreements. Other sports are strategically scheduled against D1 opponents where the intelligence system projects the partner institution as competitive or favored. Every game across every sport at every level is on KTV. The combination of ESPN+ broadcasts, KTV content, D1 competitive results, the evangelical Christian identity, and the institutional transformation narrative produces awareness that no marketing budget could replicate.

Conversion model: national media coverage generates awareness. Digital marketing and the enrollment engine convert awareness into applications. Dipson handles prospective student inquiries 24/7. Campus visits (physical at South Florida, virtual through KTV) convert applications to enrollment.

Estimated Year 1 funnel: awareness reach of 400,000+ through media and marketing > 8,000 website visits and inquiries > 1,500 applications > 700 admitted > 400 enrolled. Yield on admitted students is estimated at 55-60% given the Christian market segmentation, which filters for high-fit applicants and yields above small-college average. These numbers grow substantially in Year 2 through Year 5 as athletic results accumulate, the brand matures, and the Miami Lakes campus comes online.

CAC for brand-driven students is the most traditional marketing cost in the model. Estimated $1,200-$2,500 per enrolled student in Year 1 through digital marketing, search, social media, and content marketing. CAC is expected to decrease significantly over time as earned media from athletic performance, KTV content library, and organic brand awareness compound through the KaNeXT distribution network.

### Channel 3: Online Enrollment

The primary volume driver from Year 2 onward. Online students enroll in accredited programs delivered through the KaNeXT OS layered on the partner institution's existing Populi infrastructure.

| Metric | Y1 | Y3 | Y5 |
|---|---|---|---|
| Online students | 450 | 5,300 | 12,600 |

The online ramp is explicitly phased. Year 1 is scale-up: the partner institution's existing accredited online programs (business administration, MBA launching Fall 2025, BSN in development) expand through KaNeXT marketing infrastructure and enrollment engine operation. Year 2 through Year 3 is acceleration: additional programs approved and launched, marketing channels optimized based on Year 1 data, organic leads from the KaNeXT distribution network growing. Year 4 through Year 5 is volume: the enrollment engine is mature, athletic visibility and media coverage compound awareness, the program portfolio is broad, and organic channels represent a growing share of leads.

The partner institution's online programs are structurally differentiated from the hundreds of generic online degree offerings. Christian-values curriculum. AI-powered advising through Dipson. Integration with platform media and content through KTV and KPlay. KPay makes payment frictionless (financial aid, tuition, and campus commerce all through one wallet). Intelligence-governed retention through the RKR architecture.

The partner institution's existing online infrastructure is a real advantage. Populi has been the student information system since 2012. Online operations are already deployed, already serving 424 international students from 43 countries, already processing admissions and financial aid. KaNeXT scales what works rather than building from zero.

For context on category scale: GCU grew to approximately 98,000 online students in 17 years. Liberty reached 124,000 online students over decades. KaNeXT's Year 5 online projection of approximately 12,600 students is a fraction of that scale, achieved in compressed time because the partner institution's online infrastructure already exists and KaNeXT's distribution network accelerates reach without the hundred-million-dollar marketing budgets GCU and Liberty required to build.

Conversion model: the enrollment engine operates a professional digital marketing and call center operation. Leads are generated through search advertising, social media, content marketing, and organic platform traffic (parents and families already on the platform through KTV, athletic content, and wallet). Leads are tracked through CRM from first touchpoint through enrollment. Admissions counselors follow up within hours. Dipson provides 24/7 AI-assisted student support.

Estimated Year 1 funnel: 30,000 leads generated > 6,000 qualified prospects > 1,500 applications > 900 admitted > 450 enrolled. Lead-to-enrollment conversion of approximately 1.5%, which is within industry range for online higher education programs with room to improve through intelligence-governed optimization.

CAC for online students is estimated at $1,800-$3,500 per enrolled student in Year 1, declining to $800-$1,500 by Year 5 as organic channels (platform audience, athletic brand awareness, international network) reduce paid marketing dependency. For comparison, industry CAC for online higher education programs typically ranges from $2,000-$5,000+.

### Channel 4: International and Diaspora Pipeline

The partner institution already serves 424 international students from 43 countries. This is not a future channel. It is an existing infrastructure that KaNeXT scales through platform distribution and marketing amplification.

| Metric | Y1 | Y3 | Y5 |
|---|---|---|---|
| International and diaspora students | 500 | 2,600 | 8,500 |

The channel works through multiple overlapping networks. Miami-Dade County has among the largest Cuban, Hispanic, Caribbean, Haitian, Nigerian, and international communities in the United States. The partner institution's 40-year presence in the South Florida market and its existing pipeline of 43 nations represents an institutional asset that generic Christian colleges do not have. The founder's Nigerian-American background and the family's connection to the largest Nigerian church community in California adds diaspora reach that maps directly to evangelical Christian higher education demand.

The pipeline compounds through the platform. Students, families, and friends are on KTV watching athletic content, on KPay transacting, and on Dipson asking questions. Athletic success in sports with cultural followings in these communities (basketball, soccer, track and field) amplifies the visibility. A prospective student in Lagos or San Juan or Port-au-Prince discovers the partner institution through platform content and engages through Dipson before any admissions counselor is involved.

International and diaspora enrollment is growing organically at the partner institution already. KaNeXT accelerates the growth through platform distribution, targeted marketing, and the expanding academic program portfolio. Y5 projection of approximately 8,500 students represents roughly 20x the current 424 baseline, driven by program expansion, brand visibility, and platform network effects.

CAC for international and diaspora students is materially lower than general online CAC because the channel is largely organic and relationship-driven. Estimated $500-$1,500 per enrolled student blended across recruitment costs and referral incentives.

---

## Enrollment Intelligence Architecture

The KaNeXT Enrollment Management Intelligence governs the full student lifecycle. Every channel, every conversion rate, every retention decision, and every forecast operates through governed components rather than through traditional enrollment marketing assumptions.

**Pipeline KR (PLKR).** Forecast accuracy, demographic health, market share, and pipeline diversity. PLKR governs enrollment projections across all four channels. Forecasts are never inflated above demographic pipeline plus fifteen percent. Every projection in this document is bounded by that governance rule, which is why the Year 5 targets sit below what aggressive but ungoverned projections would claim.

**Retention KR (RKR).** Primary retention rate, trend, cohort equity, and revenue impact of attrition. RKR governs retention outcomes at the cohort level and drives intervention decisions when early-warning signals appear.

**Persistence KR (PKR).** Completion and graduation rates, pace and momentum through the curriculum, progression pipeline integrity, and completion equity across populations. PKR governs the mid-cycle and end-cycle of the student lifecycle.

**Recovery KR (RCKR).** Early alert effectiveness, stop-out recovery, academic recovery programs, and root cause analysis. RCKR governs the recovery tier of the enrollment system when retention or persistence signals break.

Traditional enrollment projections are marketing estimates. KaNeXT projections are governed outputs from an intelligence system that tracks pipeline forecasting, retention cohort analysis, persistence risk flags, and recovery effectiveness in real time. The system identifies leaking bucket patterns, cliff-ahead demographic trajectories, aid-dependent retention risks, gateway gatekeeper courses, invisible departure patterns, and retention theater before they compound into enrollment collapse. The partner institution operates with this intelligence architecture deployed from Day 1. No peer institution operates with comparable enrollment governance.

---

## Retention As A Growth Component

Retention is not a footnote. Retention is a growth channel governed by the RKR component of the Enrollment Intelligence architecture.

**Baseline.** the partner institution's current retention rate is approximately 79% per the most recent institutional reporting. This is already above the national average for small Christian colleges, reflecting strong existing institutional identity and mission alignment.

**Target trajectory.**

| Year | Target Retention Rate |
|---|---|
| Year 1 | 80% - 82% |
| Year 2 | 82% - 84% |
| Year 3 | 84% - 86% |
| Year 4 | 85% - 87% |
| Year 5 | 86% - 88% |

The trajectory reflects realistic compounding improvement driven by the intelligence architecture, expanded student services, Dipson 24/7 advising, cluster housing community effects, athletic engagement for athletes and non-athletes alike, and the institutional quality investment described in the revenue model.

**Why retention matters for the revenue math.** A one-percentage-point retention improvement on a 10,000-student cohort produces 100 additional students retained per year. Compounded across the five-year horizon, retention improvement from 79% to 86% represents approximately 2,000-3,000 additional cumulative retained students versus the baseline retention rate. That is meaningful enrollment that does not require new acquisition spending. Retention at scale is the cheapest enrollment growth available, and the RKR intelligence system targets exactly this outcome.

The institutional quality investment described in the revenue model (faculty compensation above Christian university market, academic program development, student services excellence, campus infrastructure) is directly what earns retention. Students who experience premium institutional quality return at higher rates. The retention target trajectory is grounded in the quality investment, not aspirational.

---

## Total Enrollment Reconciliation

Combining all four channels plus retention produces the total enrollment trajectory aligned to the institutional revenue model.

| Source | Y1 | Y3 | Y5 |
|---|---|---|---|
| Athletic (Varsity + Development) | 220 | 550 | 900 |
| Brand-driven (on-campus) | 400 | 2,800 | 5,000 |
| Retained existing the partner institution | 900 | 700 | 500 |
| Online | 450 | 5,300 | 12,600 |
| International and Diaspora | 500 | 2,600 | 8,500 |
| **Total on-campus** | **~1,520 - 1,720** | **~4,050 - 4,650** | **~6,400 - 7,500** |
| **Total online and international** | **~950 - 1,250** | **~7,900 - 8,800** | **~21,100 - 22,500** |
| **Grand total** | **~2,000 - 2,200** | **~10,300 - 11,700** | **~24,600 - 27,700** |

Note: channel numbers are approximate and include blended attribution (a student driven by athletic visibility who enrolls in an online program counts in the online channel, for example). Totals align with the Institutional Revenue Model ranges.

---

## Unit Economics by Channel

Approximate unit economics at Year 3, using the Year 3 tuition average of $11,000-$12,500 for residential and $7,000-$8,000 blended for online.

| Channel | Y3 CAC | Y3 Revenue per Student (KaNeXT share 60%) | Y3 LTV (3-year blended) |
|---|---|---|---|
| Athletic (on-campus) | ~$300 embedded | $7,500 tuition + $8,000 housing/board = $15,500 | $46,500 |
| Brand (on-campus) | $800 - $1,500 | $15,500 | $46,500 |
| Online | $1,200 - $2,500 | $4,800 (60% of $8,000 avg tuition) | $14,400 |
| International/Diaspora | $400 - $1,000 | $4,800 blended | $14,400 |

On-campus students generate substantially higher per-student revenue than online students because KaNeXT receives 100% of housing, dining, and campus commerce at Miami Lakes in addition to tuition share. Online students generate lower per-student revenue but at higher margin (minimal incremental delivery cost per student) and with no physical infrastructure requirements.

The enrollment model is designed so that athletic and brand-driven on-campus enrollment anchors the early years (high revenue per student, high visibility, high institutional impact) while online enrollment drives the scale (high volume, high margin, infrastructure-light). International and diaspora enrollment provides organic-cost growth that compounds through the platform distribution network.

---

## Key Assumptions and Sensitivities

**Channel certainty varies significantly.** Athletic recruiting is the channel closest to direct institutional control because it is driven by scholarship offers, intelligence-backed targeting, and the multi-level team structure. Brand-driven on-campus enrollment and online enrollment are more modeled and market-dependent, relying on media visibility, marketing effectiveness, and competitive positioning. International and diaspora enrollment is organic and relationship-driven but its pace is the hardest to predict with precision.

**Enrollment pace is the single most important assumption in the institutional revenue model.** If enrollment grows slower than projected, revenue declines faster than costs can be reduced. The intelligence architecture (PLKR, RKR, PKR, RCKR) is specifically designed to detect pace deterioration early and trigger intervention before small variance compounds into large shortfalls.

**Online enrollment is the most assumption-sensitive channel.** The Year 5 projection of approximately 12,600 online students is achievable given GCU and Liberty precedent but requires successful online program launches (accreditation and faculty approval), effective digital marketing at scale, competitive tuition pricing, and sustained brand momentum from athletic visibility and platform distribution.

**Athletic enrollment is the most predictable channel** because it is directly controlled through the intelligence system and scholarship offers. If the athletic system deploys as described, 220-900 athletes across Year 1 to Year 5 is realistic based on scholarship capacity and recruiting infrastructure.

**Retention compounds.** A 1% annual retention improvement across a growing enrollment base compounds significantly over five years. The retention target trajectory (79% baseline to 86-88% by Year 5) is grounded in the RKR intelligence architecture and the institutional quality investment that supports it. Retention below target reduces cumulative enrollment. Retention above target compounds enrollment favorably.

**CAC assumptions are modeled, not validated.** Actual CAC will depend on market conditions, competitive dynamics, and the effectiveness of the enrollment operation. CAC is expected to decline over time as organic channels grow through platform distribution, but the rate of decline is uncertain.

---

## Disclaimer

All enrollment projections are forward-looking estimates. Actual enrollment depends on marketing effectiveness, program approval timelines, competitive dynamics, retention outcomes, brand development, market conditions, and operational execution. The conversion rates and CAC estimates in this document are modeled assumptions governed by the intelligence architecture but not yet validated against live operations at the partner institution. The retention trajectory depends on the institutional quality investment materializing as described and on student experience outcomes at both South Florida and Miami Lakes campus locations. Channel mix may shift materially based on early results, with the intelligence architecture triggering reallocation toward the channels producing strongest yield and lowest CAC.


<!-- ============================================================ -->
<!-- FILE: 04_The_Institution/athlete_economy.md -->
<!-- ============================================================ -->

# Athlete Economy: Recruiting, NIL, and Representation

**KaNeXT LLC | Confidential**

---

## The Platform Is the Ecosystem

This is the fundamental difference between KaNeXT and every recruiting service, NIL platform, and scouting tool on the market. KaNeXT is not a recruiting product added to the athletic landscape. The platform IS the athletic landscape.

Every mandate institution and network school operates on KaNeXT. Every team is on the platform. Every game is filmed, streamed, and processed. Every player has a profile. Every coach has access. The recruiting database is not something athletes sign up for and hope coaches visit. It is the operating system that coaches already use every day to run their programs.

When a coach opens KaNeXT to manage their roster, prepare for a game, or review film, they are already inside the recruiting ecosystem. When a parent downloads the app to watch their child play on KTV, their child is already visible to every coach on the platform. There is no separate recruiting product to buy, no separate database to register for, and no separate film service to upload to. The OS is the recruiting tool.

---

## Why Existing Recruiting Services Are Structurally Limited

NCSA charges families $3,000-$6,000 for a profile and coach contacts. The athlete uploads a highlight reel, fills out academic information, and gets access to a database of college programs. That is a directory. NCSA does not evaluate the athlete. It does not know what level the athlete should compete at. It does not know which schools need the athlete's specific skill set. It does not process game film through an intelligence system. It does not compute system fit. It does not project development trajectory. It connects names to names and hopes for a match.

KaNeXT operates at a fundamentally different level because the intelligence system does what directories and point tools cannot:

**Level identification.** Every athlete on the platform has a KR (0-100) computed through the full intelligence pipeline. The KR is cross-level normalized through KLVN, meaning a player's rating is directly comparable across NAIA, D2, D1, and professional levels. The athlete does not have to guess where they belong. The intelligence provides a structured answer.

**Development guidance.** The KR decomposes into component KRs (offensive, defensive, tools, basketball IQ, and sport-specific equivalents). The athlete sees which traits are strong, which are weak, and what specific improvements would raise their overall rating and open up the next competitive level.

**School matching.** The system fit computation matches an athlete's trait profile against the specific needs of every program on the platform. A point guard who excels in pick-and-roll and spacing sees the programs whose offensive systems require exactly those traits. This is not a list of "schools that offer your sport." It is a ranked list of programs where the athlete's specific profile fills a specific need.

**Pro pathway.** The pro KR shows the gap between where the athlete is today and where professional competition begins. The system identifies the specific skill improvements that would close that gap. Development becomes targeted, measurable, and connected to real outcomes.

---

## Two-Way Intelligence Matching

The intelligence works in both directions simultaneously.

**For athletes:** the platform shows what level you should target, which schools need your exact profile, what you need to improve, and what your ceiling looks like with development. This is available to every athlete on the platform at no cost. A player at a mandate institution in rural Oklahoma has the same intelligence-backed profile and the same visibility to every coach on the platform as a player at a major prep school in Los Angeles.

**For coaches:** the platform shows every player in the national pool who fits their specific system need. A D2 coach looking for a wing who can space the floor and switch defensively in their 4-out motion offense does not call everyone they know. They ask Dipson. The intelligence returns a ranked list of players across all levels whose trait profiles match that specific need, sorted by system fit, KR, academic eligibility, and geographic proximity. The coach sees film from every game the player has ever played on the platform, processed by KVision with intelligence overlay.

This two-way matching democratizes recruiting. It removes the geography bias (coaches only recruit who they see). It removes the visibility bias (athletes at small schools are invisible). It removes the network bias (connections matter more than talent). The intelligence connects the right athletes to the right programs based on what actually matters: traits, system fit, development trajectory, and institutional need.

---

## The Transfer Portal

The transfer portal is currently chaos. Athletes enter the portal with no structured intelligence about where they fit. Coaches scramble to evaluate portal entrants with incomplete information on short timelines. Decisions are reactive, not strategic.

On KaNeXT, when a player enters the portal, the intelligence system immediately computes their system fit against every program on the platform. Coaches see which portal entrants would improve their Team KR, how each addition changes projected outcomes, and whether the player's trait profile fills a specific roster gap. Athletes see which programs need their profile and where they project to have the most impact. The portal becomes an intelligence-governed marketplace rather than a scramble.

---

## NIL: Intelligence-Connected Compensation

The current NIL landscape is disconnected from player value. Athletes with large social media followings get deals. Athletes who are more valuable on the court but less visible online often do not. There is no structural connection between what a player is worth to their team and what they are compensated.

KaNeXT connects NIL to intelligence. The Player Transfer Value (PTV) computation shows what each player is worth to their specific team based on their KR, system fit contribution, and replaceability. Coaches see which players are underpaid relative to their PTV, which flags portal risk before it materializes. NIL allocation becomes strategic rather than arbitrary.

**The NIL marketplace** connects brands, agents, and companies to athletes through intelligence. A brand in Business mode does not browse a generic list of athletes. They search by KR, archetype, sport, school, audience metrics, and engagement data. The intelligence helps brands find athletes whose profile actually matches their campaign objectives. Deal terms are proposed through Dipson. Compliance checks are automatic. Contracts are governed. Payments settle through KPay. Content deliverables are tracked. The compliance officer has full visibility. No external platform touches the transaction.

**The merch store** gives every athlete a zero-cost way to monetize their brand. Branded merchandise produced through the fulfillment center. The athlete chooses designs. KaNeXT handles production, shipping, and customer service. Revenue share flows directly to the athlete's wallet.

**Fan contributions** connect supporters directly to athletes. Fans stake NIL money to specific players. Play-based pledges ("$2 per three-pointer") auto-charge via KPay using StatKeeper data. All funds governed through the settlement chain with full compliance documentation.

Every NIL transaction follows the same governed settlement chain as every other financial action on the platform: Event, Rules, Authorization, Payment, Settlement, Audit. The compliance failures that dominate the current NIL landscape are structurally prevented because the governance is architectural, not procedural.

---

## Camps, Tournaments, and Events

The intelligence system enriches every competitive event on the platform.

Travel ball leagues, AAU/SSB events, showcases, and camps currently operate without structured evaluation. Coaches attend, watch, take notes, and make subjective assessments. Parents pay thousands of dollars for exposure with no guarantee their child will be properly evaluated.

When these events operate on KaNeXT or when KaNeXT runs its own tournament properties, every game is filmed, processed, and evaluated. Tournament directors distribute packets to attending coaches with KR data on every participant. A college coach at a showcase receives intelligence-backed evaluations rather than rosters and box scores.

For parents: the camp or tournament becomes a legitimate evaluation event, not just exposure. Their child leaves with an updated KR, specific development feedback, and visibility to every coach on the platform. They can see what their child needs to improve to reach the next level. The pro KR shows the gap to professional competition.

For coaches: the event is pre-scouted by intelligence. They arrive knowing which players match their system needs and can focus their time on the highest-fit prospects rather than watching every game hoping to find someone.

For event operators: KaNeXT's intelligence, cameras, and streaming infrastructure make their event more valuable to every participant. Events on the platform attract more athletes and more coaches because the intelligence adds a layer that no other event can offer.

---

## Representation at Scale

Traditional sports agencies sign athletes one by one, take 3-10% commission, and rely on personal relationships. The largest agencies represent a few hundred clients across all sports.

KaNeXT has athletes across mandate institutions and network schools evaluated, profiled, and projectable as the platform deploys. The intelligence system projects where each athlete lands on the pro KR scale. The development pathway shows best-fit professional leagues and the specific trait improvements that would shift the projection. The athlete and their family build a relationship with the platform over years of accurate evaluation and development guidance.

When representation is needed, KaNeXT is positioned as a natural option because the intelligence, the relationship, and the data already live inside the same system. The scale advantage is structural: the platform evaluates the population continuously rather than recruiting clients one at a time. Representation services, if offered, would operate at industry-standard commission rates on contracts informed by governed intelligence.

---

## The Career Arc

The athlete economy is designed around the full career, not a single transaction.

A parent downloads the app to watch their child play at a mandate institution. The child is evaluated. The intelligence shows their level, their strengths, their development areas, and which programs need their profile. The family uses this to make informed decisions about the next step. The athlete transfers or is recruited to a program that fits their traits and system. Their KR updates as they develop. NIL opportunities appear as their profile grows. The merch store launches. Brand deals come through the marketplace. The athlete competes in higher-level competition. Pro projections appear. Representation conversations begin.

Every stage happens inside one platform. Every transaction settles through one wallet. Every evaluation uses one intelligence system. The continuity from first game to professional contract is the competitive advantage that no collection of separate recruiting services, film platforms, NIL marketplaces, and agencies can replicate.

---

## Competitive Position

Every existing player in this space serves one function. Recruiting databases (NCSA, PrepStar) provide profiles without intelligence. Film platforms (Hudl) provide video without evaluation. NIL platforms (Opendorse, INFLCR) facilitate deals without compliance governance or connection to on-court value. Agencies provide representation without the data that informed the evaluation. Merch platforms provide fulfillment without institutional integration.

KaNeXT provides all of these simultaneously because they are all expressions of the same underlying intelligence architecture, settlement layer, and institutional operating system. The athletes are already on the platform. The coaches are already on the platform. The games are already filmed. The intelligence is already computed. The wallet is already active. There is nothing to sign up for, nothing to pay for, and nothing to integrate. The athlete economy is the natural consequence of the platform operating as designed.

---

## Revenue

The athlete economy monetizes through the existing platform layers: 5% commerce on merch, tickets, and NIL-related transactions. KPay settlement on every financial action. KTV subscriptions and advertising driven by athlete content. Learning Lab subscriptions for coaches using recruiting intelligence. Data licensing for scouts and analytics companies. Agency commissions on professional representation at industry-standard rates. Tournament and event hosting revenue.

Revenue model details are documented separately.


<!-- ============================================================ -->
<!-- FILE: 04_The_Institution/tournament_event_properties.md -->
<!-- ============================================================ -->

# Tournament and Event Properties

**KaNeXT LLC | Confidential**

---

## Overview

KaNeXT owns and operates tournament and event properties across sports. These are not sponsorship activations or one-off showcases. They are owned institutional events where every KaNeXT engine is active, every dollar flows through governed settlement, every fan is inside the app, and every game feeds the intelligence system and content library simultaneously.

The events serve three functions. They are proof events (the entire KaNeXT ecosystem operating live on a national stage). They are content events (multi-day productions feeding KTV, generating broadcast value, and compounding the media library). And they are distribution events (concentrated app installs from every participating team's fanbase, every broadcast viewer, and every family following the action).

Basketball is the first sport because the intelligence deployment is deepest. The model is portable to any sport once the infrastructure is proven.

---

## The Postseason Tournament

Every year, hundreds of college basketball teams finish their season without a postseason invitation. D1 teams that narrowly missed the NCAA Tournament (Power Conference programs, strong mid-majors, conference tournament losers with 20-win seasons). Lower-division champions whose seasons ended without a national stage against higher-level competition. D2 champions, D3 champions, NAIA champions have never had the opportunity to play D1 competition in a structured, high-stakes environment.

KaNeXT builds the postseason that does not currently exist.

The tournament is a bracket format with escalating prize money at every round. The champion receives a package significant enough to meaningfully impact a program's recruiting budget. Athletic directors have incentive to participate because the money is real, the exposure is national through KTV and broadcast partnerships, and the competition is legitimate.

The competitive structure creates something unprecedented: cross-division competition where the intelligence system provides the analytical framework. KR ratings, team evaluations, and simulation projections give the tournament a data-driven narrative layer that traditional postseason events lack. Every game validates (or challenges) the intelligence publicly. The tournament becomes a live test of the evaluation system in front of a national audience.

The tournament starts with basketball. Once the model is proven, it extends to every sport where teams finish without a postseason invitation and lower-division champions have no opportunity to compete against higher-level programs. Soccer, baseball, volleyball, softball, football: the structure is repeatable.

---

## Showcase and Cultural Events

Separate from the postseason tournament, KaNeXT owns regular-season showcase events designed to put the partner institution and KaNeXT-powered teams on a national stage alongside cultural programming.

**Memorial Day Weekend Showcase.** Early-summer event featuring competitive games alongside community programming. D1 teams invited for guarantee-game-style competition. Faith-based community celebration, entrepreneurship programming, community engagement. ESPN+ broadcast potential through participating D1 media agreements.

**MLK Weekend Classic.** Mid-season event built around the Martin Luther King Jr. holiday. Cross-division competition. Cultural and community programming. National broadcast window during a weekend when college basketball has significant viewership.

**Additional properties** are developed as the calendar and the sports portfolio expand. Any sport where a marquee matchup generates media value and puts KaNeXT-powered teams on a national stage is a candidate for an owned event property.

These events are not just basketball games. The cultural programming, community engagement, and institutional storytelling generate media value beyond the athletic competition. The combination of high-level competition and mission-aligned programming produces more coverage and more brand value than either would alone.

---

## How the Events Operate

Every KaNeXT-owned event runs on the full platform.

**Intelligence.** The KaNeXT intelligence engines are active across every game: evaluating players, computing team strength, updating the 250,000+ athlete database, governing pregame through postgame intelligence, publishing simulations, and tracking development. The event is not just entertainment. It is a live intelligence operation producing structured data from every possession.

**Settlement.** All financial activity flows through governed settlement. Ticketing, sponsorship, broadcast revenue, vendor contracts, concessions, merchandise, travel stipends, and prize payouts all follow the canonical settlement chain with revenue splits defined before the first game tips.

**Content.** All games are broadcast live through KTV. Highlights generated automatically. Content enters the permanent library. Every game from every KaNeXT-owned event is available on-demand indefinitely, building a growing archive of high-level competition with intelligence overlay.

**Distribution.** All fans are inside the app. Tickets purchased through KPay. Merch through the fulfillment center. Brackets, KR ratings, and simulation projections accessible in-app. Every fan who attends or watches a KaNeXT-owned event is a potential platform user with one tap to a wallet, a phone number, and a merch store.

---

## Revenue Streams

Each event generates revenue across multiple channels simultaneously.

Ticket sales and gate revenue. Sponsorship and partnership deals. Broadcasting rights (KTV plus external broadcast partners). Merchandise sales through the fulfillment center. Concessions and vendor fees. Prize money underwriting (structured so that prize payouts are funded from event revenue, not fund capital). Every dollar settles through KPay.

The events are designed to be self-sustaining or profitable from Year 1 because the infrastructure (KTV, KPay, intelligence, fulfillment, cameras) already exists. The marginal cost of producing an event on existing infrastructure is substantially lower than building event infrastructure from scratch.

---

## Strategic Value

The tournament and event properties are not standalone businesses. They are proof, content, and distribution concentrated into multi-day productions.

**Proof.** The intelligence system makes public predictions. The tournament produces public outcomes. Every result that matches the intelligence projections builds credibility. Every upset that the intelligence flagged as possible builds credibility. The events are the highest-visibility demonstration of what the intelligence system can do.

**Content.** A multi-day tournament produces dozens of games across multiple days. Each game feeds KTV, the intelligence system, and the content library. A single tournament weekend can produce more content than many programs produce in an entire season.

**Distribution.** Every participating team brings its fanbase. Every fanbase downloads the app to watch, buy tickets, follow brackets, and purchase merch. A 16-team tournament with average fanbases of 5,000-10,000 supporters per team delivers 80,000-160,000 potential app installs in a single weekend.

**Intelligence validation.** The tournament produces outcomes against which the intelligence system's predictions can be publicly measured. This is the live proving ground for the KR system, the simulation engine, and the evaluation methodology.

---

## Annual Calendar

The tournament and event properties create a year-round calendar of KaNeXT-owned programming.

Early-season showcases (November-December). Mid-season cultural events (January MLK weekend). Regular-season D1 guarantee games throughout conference play. Late-season pre-tournament positioning events. Postseason tournament (March-April, after conference championships).

The calendar expands as KaNeXT adds sports. Each sport follows the same infrastructure model once proven through basketball.

---

## Disclaimer

All event descriptions are forward-looking. Actual event scheduling, participation, broadcasting agreements, prize structures, and revenue depend on institutional partnerships, broadcast market conditions, competitive scheduling, and execution.

<!-- FILE: 04_The_Institution/campus_land_deep_dive.md -->
