# Banking Infrastructure Strategy

**KaNeXT LLC | Confidential**

---

## Purpose

This document explains how the financial rails behind KPay are structured, the phased approach from third-party banking-as-a-service to owned bank charter, and the Year 2+ bank acquisition capital event thesis. Banking infrastructure is a Year 2+ concern, not a Year 1 priority. KPay operates from Day 1 on a BaaS partnership. An owned bank charter is a future capital event raised separately from the Year 1 $33M partnership raise.

---

## The Strategic Case for Owning a Charter

KPay is the financial layer of the KaNeXT OS. Every transaction on the platform - tuition, housing, dining, merch, tickets, donations, NIL, remittance, concessions - flows through KPay.

Without an owned charter, KPay operates through a white-label banking-as-a-service (BaaS) partner. The BaaS partner holds the charter, controls the terms, sets the fees, and can change or terminate the relationship. KaNeXT captures the software margin but not the banking margin. The BaaS partner sits between KaNeXT and the financial infrastructure that powers every transaction on the platform.

With an owned charter, KaNeXT controls the financial infrastructure directly. Tuition processing, financial aid disbursement, payroll, vendor payments, remittance, lending, deposit management, and institutional capital flows all move through infrastructure KaNeXT owns. Dependency on a single third-party partner is eliminated. The banking margin (deposit spread, lending income, interchange) is captured internally rather than shared with a BaaS provider.

At the transaction volumes the mandate and institutional network are designed to produce, the difference between BaaS margin and owned-charter margin is substantial recurring revenue.

---

## Phase 1: BaaS (Year 1 and Early Year 2)

KPay does not require an owned charter to launch. Phase 1 financial operations run through a BaaS partnership.

**Wallet operations.** KPay wallets activate through a BaaS partner. Students, parents, fans, and institutional users hold balances, make transactions, and receive payments through the wallet. The user experience is identical whether the underlying charter is owned or partnered.

**Commerce processing.** Flat processing on all institutional transactions. Card-present and card-not-present. Wallet-to-wallet closed-loop transactions. Standard card-network processing infrastructure handles the underlying rails.

**Basic remittance.** International transfers through partnered remittance infrastructure. Lower fees than legacy providers but not yet at the full margin capture of an owned charter.

**Institutional financial operations.** Tuition payment, financial aid disbursement, campus commerce, dining, parking: all processing through KPay from Day 1, backed by the BaaS charter.

The BaaS phase is fully functional. It is not a degraded experience. The difference is margin capture and strategic independence, not user-facing capability.

---

## Phase 2: Bank Acquisition (Year 2+ Capital Event)

The bank acquisition is a Year 2+ capital event raised separately from the Year 1 partnership funding. Acquisition capital is sized and structured at the time of the capital event based on Year 1 operational results, the specific acquisition target's condition at that time, and regulatory conditions.

### The Acquisition Target

KaNeXT has identified a Miami-based community bank as the primary acquisition target. The target is FDIC-insured, federally chartered, and carries several structural characteristics that make it an unusually good fit: sub-$100M asset base with branch infrastructure in Miami-Dade County, distressed ownership profile that creates a motivated-seller dynamic, and a regulatory posture that allows a clean change-of-control path. Additional details on the specific target, including operational metrics, ownership structure, and litigation context, are available under a non-disclosure framework during diligence conversations.

The target's distressed position creates the acquisition opportunity. The ownership profile creates the motivation for a clean exit. KaNeXT's deposit commitments, regulatory cleanup, and structural rebuild create the value the seller cannot create alone. The specifics of the target are withheld from this summary document to preserve negotiating posture and because naming identifying details publicly would compromise the acquisition.

### Acquisition Structure

A nominal upfront purchase consideration structure may be feasible if deposit commitments, regulatory approval, and transaction cleanup provide sufficient value to the current ownership.

KaNeXT would commit approximately $50M to $100M in new deposits through its institutional network, including the partner institution institutional and student accounts under the Full IOA, KPay wallet balances across the broader platform user base, mandate institution commerce settlement through GAAC and expanded governing body partnerships, remittance float through cross-border corridors, and fund and institutional capital flows.

The current ownership receives a clean exit. KaNeXT receives a fully chartered, FDIC-insured national bank with existing branch infrastructure, lending capabilities, and regulatory approvals.

The specific terms of any acquisition are subject to negotiation, regulatory approval, and completion of due diligence.

### Post-Acquisition Plan

**Rebrand.** The existing brand carries reputational baggage from the ownership litigation. A clean rebrand signals new ownership, new direction, and alignment with the KaNeXT institutional ecosystem.

**Apply for MDI designation.** The acquired institution may qualify for Minority Depository Institution status through minority governance composition and community-service criteria. MDI designation is administered by the FDIC based on actual ownership, governance, and community-service profile. KaNeXT's founder composition and planned majority-minority board structure provide a credible MDI path independent of any specific institutional partnership. If MDI status is granted, it activates federal capital programs, corporate deposit flows, and CRA-motivated lending from major banks.

**Reconstitute the board.** Current board members associated with the existing ownership structure will be replaced with a majority-minority board appointed by KaNeXT. Board composition will satisfy both MDI governance requirements and KaNeXT's institutional operating standards.

**Capitalize.** Approximately $10M in initial capital. This is a balance sheet asset (it sits in the bank's capital account), not a sunk cost. It expands the bank's lending capacity and satisfies regulatory capital requirements for the planned growth trajectory. Additional capital may be accessible through federal capital programs discussed below.

**Integrate with KPay.** The bank charter provides the regulated depository infrastructure underlying KPay wallet operations. Student accounts, institutional deposits, remittance processing, and settlement activity transition from the BaaS partner to the owned charter. The user experience does not change. The margin capture changes substantially.

**Pursue CDFI certification.** Community Development Financial Institutions Fund certification is pursued if the institution's post-close business model, target-market focus, and community accountability satisfy the certification requirements. CDFI certification opens additional federal funding programs including CDFI Fund Financial Assistance awards and the Bond Guarantee Program.

**Evaluate federal capital programs.** Programs such as the Emergency Capital Investment Program have historically provided capital to qualifying institutions at favorable terms. ECIP's original application window closed in January 2023 but illustrates the type of federal capital program that can materially improve bank economics. Future programs or reopened windows are pursued as they become available.

**Open branches at institutional network campuses.** the partner institution's South Florida campus and the Miami Lakes flagship (upon development) become branch locations. Each institution added to the KaNeXT institutional network over time represents a potential branch opportunity inside a captive institutional environment with an embedded, structurally recurring account base.

---

## Regulatory Process

### FDIC Change of Control

The acquisition requires FDIC approval through the change-of-control application process. The FDIC evaluates the acquiring party's financial capacity, management competence, compliance history, and the impact on the institution's safety and soundness.

**Pre-filing meeting.** Engage the FDIC and OCC (for national charters) before submitting the formal application. The pre-filing meeting identifies potential concerns and shapes the application.

**Application submission.** Formal change-of-control application with supporting documentation: acquirer financial statements, business plan, management team qualifications, deposit growth projections, capital plan, and compliance infrastructure.

**Review timeline.** 6-12 months minimum from application to approval. Distressed ownership situations can materially extend the timeline if the regulator requires additional diligence, conditions the approval on full resolution of outstanding matters, or requires enhanced compliance commitments as a condition of transfer. The target's specific profile is evaluated against these factors during the pre-filing engagement.

**Conditions.** The FDIC may impose conditions on approval: enhanced compliance monitoring, capital maintenance requirements, business plan milestones, or periodic reporting. Conditions are manageable and expected for a change-of-control transaction involving a distressed institution.

### State and Federal Coordination

The target bank is OCC-regulated as a national charter, making the OCC the primary regulator. MDI designation is administered by the FDIC. CDFI certification is administered by the Treasury Department's CDFI Fund. Each application path runs in parallel post-closing.

---

## Deposit Growth Trajectory

Deposits are generated structurally across the institutional network, platform user base, governing body mandate, and fund capital flows.

**Institutional Network:** the partner institution student wallet accounts, faculty and staff accounts, institutional operating accounts, payroll deposits, and tuition flow. Each institution added to the network over time adds a complete set of institutional and individual deposit accounts.

**Governing Body Mandate:** commerce settlement deposits across mandate institutions, growing as governing body agreements are executed and transaction volume scales.

**Platform:** KPay wallet balances from all users regardless of institutional affiliation, remittance float from international transfers, and interchange settlement.

**Fund and Institutional Capital:** fund capital under management, management fee flow, philanthropic capital in institutional accounts, and future institutional capital events.

**CRA Corporate Deposits:** major banks depositing into the MDI to satisfy regulatory obligations, independent of KaNeXT's own customer acquisition, upon MDI designation.

Estimated deposit trajectory:

| Year | Estimated Deposits | Primary Sources |
|---|---|---|
| Y1 | n/a | BaaS phase, no owned charter |
| Y2 | $50M-$75M (post-acquisition) | the partner institution accounts, fund capital, early wallet adoption, initial mandate commerce |
| Y3 | $150M-$250M | Institutional network expansion, growing wallet base, CRA deposits, remittance float |
| Y4 | $300M-$500M | Mandate fully deployed, significant wallet adoption, lending portfolio growth |
| Y5 | $500M-$1B+ | Mature institutional network, full mandate commerce, diaspora remittance scale |

These estimates assume Year 2 acquisition execution. Actual results depend on acquisition timing, regulatory approval, mandate deployment pace, institutional network expansion, and wallet adoption rates.

---

## Revenue Streams (Post-Charter)

Revenue is generated through five primary channels once the owned charter is operational.

**Deposit spread income.** The difference between what the bank earns on deposits (federal funds rate or equivalent) and what it pays wallet holders. Pure margin that scales linearly with deposit volume.

**Lending income.** Student loans, SBA-guaranteed loans, commercial lending, real estate lending (cluster housing and Miami Lakes development), and personal lending. Platform-native underwriting data (transaction history, income patterns, account tenure) enables better credit decisions than traditional banks can make without this data.

**Interchange income.** 1-2% on every KaNeXT Card transaction outside the ecosystem (Apple Pay, Google Pay, physical card). Monetizes every dollar a user spends anywhere.

**Treasury and institutional services.** Cash management, payroll processing, accounts payable for institutions on the platform. Each institution becomes a corporate banking client.

**CRA-motivated corporate deposits.** Major banks seeking Community Reinvestment Act credit deposit funds into MDI-designated banks at favorable terms upon MDI designation. These deposits expand the deposit base without marketing cost.

**Transaction processing.** Every KPay settlement event generates processing revenue. At mandate-scale transaction volumes, this is substantial recurring revenue.

Bank revenue is excluded from the Institutional Revenue Model. All bank revenue is additional upside.

---

## Fallback: If the Acquisition Fails

If the specific target bank cannot be acquired (regulatory denial, ownership cannot reach agreement, diligence reveals disqualifying issues), KaNeXT has multiple alternative paths.

**Alternative acquisition target.** There are approximately 150 MDI-designated banks in the United States and additional distressed-seller community banks in the broader market. The acquisition intelligence system evaluates alternative targets against the same criteria.

**De novo charter application.** Apply for a new bank charter through the OCC (national) or state regulator. De novo charter applications are more time-intensive (18-36 months) but provide a clean start without legacy issues. De novo applications have increased in recent years as regulators have become more receptive.

**Extended BaaS period.** Continue operating KPay through the BaaS partnership for an extended period. This is the default if neither acquisition nor de novo charter is achievable on the desired timeline. KPay remains fully functional. The margin capture is lower but the user experience and platform economics continue.

The bank charter is strategically important but not operationally blocking. KPay launches and operates on Day 1 through BaaS regardless of charter status. The charter improves economics. It does not enable them.

---

## Due Diligence Priorities (At Time of Acquisition)

Regulatory standing: current federal examination status, any outstanding consent orders, enforcement actions, or compliance deficiencies. Any ownership-related legal or regulatory exposure must be fully understood and resolved or mitigated prior to closing.

Asset quality: loan portfolio composition, non-performing asset ratios, and loss reserves. Any material contingent liabilities must be identified during due diligence.

Operational infrastructure: core banking systems, compliance architecture, staffing, and technology. Integration cost and timeline with KPay must be assessed prior to closing.

Regulatory approval path: OCC and FDIC change-of-control approval timeline, any conditions or commitments required, and MDI designation eligibility confirmation.

---

## Disclaimer

All descriptions of the banking infrastructure strategy are forward-looking. The specific target, terms, regulatory outcome, and timeline depend on negotiation, due diligence, FDIC/OCC approval, and resolution of the current ownership's legal situation. The bank acquisition is a Year 2+ capital event not funded by the Year 1 $33M raise. This document does not guarantee that the acquisition will be completed or that MDI, CDFI, or ECIP designations will be obtained.

<!-- ============================================================ -->

# Camera Company Acquisition Brief

**KaNeXT LLC | Confidential**

---

## Overview

KaNeXT is acquiring an American camera manufacturing company to produce the hardware that powers the mandate and the school network. The cameras are not smart devices. They are high-quality capture instruments that feed into KVision's cloud-based processing layer. Intelligence lives in the software, not the hardware. This allows aggressive pricing, no recurring subscription fees, and instant performance improvement across the entire deployed base whenever KVision's models are updated.

The camera is not the product. It is the physical entry point into the KaNeXT ecosystem. Each deployed camera becomes a capture node that simultaneously streams on KTV and feeds structured data to the intelligence system through KVision.

Budget allocation: $10M.

---

## The Product Line

Four tiers designed for different institutional needs and use cases. All tiers: American-made, waterproof IP65+ (indoor and outdoor), no recurring subscription fees, KVision AI processing included, live streaming to KTV included. Every competitor charges monthly subscriptions on top of hardware cost. KaNeXT does not.

**Cam Sport** - $200 retail (approximately $130 COGS). The mandate camera. Two provided free to every mandate institution, one indoor, one outdoor. 4K wide-angle lens with full-court and full-field coverage. WiFi upload with SD card backup. 8-hour battery life. Designed for simplicity and reliability. No on-device AI, which keeps cost minimal and hardware durable. This camera covers every sport the school offers. Comparable entry-level competitors start at $500+ with monthly subscriptions.

**Cam Sport+** - $500 retail, no recurring subscription fee. Mid-tier upgrade adding on-device AI auto-tracking (pan/tilt/zoom follows the action), 4K/60fps, 120+ degree ultra-wide angle lens, real-time score overlay syncing with the KaNeXT mobile box score, and auto-highlight generation. Competes with Pixellot Air NXT (~$949 + ~$118/month) and Hudl Focus Flex (~$999 + ~$100/month) based on publicly available pricing. Estimated three-year savings: up to $4,697.

**Cam Broadcast** - $1,500 retail, no recurring subscription fee. Broadcast-quality camera for programs that want professional production value. Multi-camera angle support, broadcast-grade output, and integration with KTV's production tools for live multi-angle switching. Competes with Pixellot Show (~$3,000-$5,000 + ~$225/month license) based on publicly available pricing. Estimated three-year savings: up to $10,600.

**Cam Pro** - $3,000+ retail, no recurring subscription fee. The premium tier for programs and venues that need permanent installation, multi-camera arrays, and the highest resolution capture for both broadcast and KVision intelligence processing.

---

## Why KaNeXT Owns Manufacturing

**Supply chain control.** The mandate requires deploying 2,000+ cameras to 1,000+ member institutions and select HS programs. Relying on a third-party manufacturer creates dependency risk. Ownership eliminates production delays, quality inconsistencies, and strategic disagreements during the mandate rollout.

**Pricing control.** Ownership allows KaNeXT to set aggressive retail prices and absorb the COGS on free mandate units without margin pressure from an external manufacturer.

**American-made and data sovereignty.** Ownership ensures the entire supply chain (design, manufacturing, assembly, and data infrastructure) remains domestic. Most competing sports cameras are manufactured overseas. These devices are deployed inside schools, churches, and institutional networks where they connect to systems that process student records, financial transactions, and personal communications. KaNeXT cameras connect to an institutional OS that handles identity, payments, phone numbers, and intelligence data. Domestic manufacturing reduces data sovereignty risk and positions KaNeXT favorably for institutional partnerships, government-adjacent contracts, and any environment where data security requirements apply.

**Hardware obsolescence eliminated.** Competitor cameras embed AI and analytics into hardware, driving up manufacturing costs and creating planned obsolescence. When the AI model improves, the camera needs to be replaced. KaNeXT cameras are capture devices. All intelligent processing happens through KVision in the cloud. No hardware replacement is required for schools to benefit from improved processing and model performance across the installed base.

**The tradeoff.** Owning manufacturing adds operational complexity: working capital, inventory management, production staffing, and quality control. This is a deliberate choice. The alternative (relying on a third-party manufacturer for 3,200+ units on a mandate timeline with aggressive pricing requirements) creates dependency risk that outweighs the operational cost of ownership.

---

## Mandate Deployment

The mandate deploys two free Cam Sports per school covering one indoor venue and one outdoor venue. The mandate covers every sport the school offers, not a single sport. A school with basketball, volleyball, soccer, baseball, and track uses the same cameras across all sports.

At approximately $130 COGS per unit, equipping the full 1,000+ institution footprint with two cameras each costs under $350,000 in hardware. The cameras are the cheapest part of the deployment. The value they create (KTV content, KVision data, platform adoption, commerce activity) is orders of magnitude larger than the hardware cost.

---

## The Upgrade Path

Schools with more than two venues, or wanting dedicated cameras per sport, or seeking enhanced capabilities purchase upgrades. Each upgrade deepens platform integration, improves data quality feeding KVision and the intelligence engines, and drives additional commerce volume through the ecosystem.

The progression is natural: a school starts with two free Cam Sports, sees the value of automated streaming and stat generation, and upgrades to Cam Sport+ for auto-tracking or Cam Broadcast for professional production quality. Every upgrade is incremental revenue with zero additional platform onboarding cost because the school is already inside the KaNeXT ecosystem.

---

## Competitive Position

KaNeXT's business model does not depend on camera margin or subscription revenue. The 5% commerce processing across the ecosystem funds the platform. This allows camera pricing that subscription-dependent competitors cannot match. Competitors cannot adopt this pricing model without destroying the subscription revenue that sustains their businesses.

---

## Strategic Value

The camera is the physical manifestation of the mandate. It is what a school receives on day one. It is what makes KTV content possible. It is what feeds KVision data. It is what creates the parent-downloads-the-app moment.

Cameras capture. KVision processes. KTV distributes. The intelligence engines evaluate. KPay monetizes. The camera is the first link in the chain that makes every other layer valuable.


<!-- ============================================================ -->
<!-- FILE: 07_Strategic_Assets/voip_communications_brief.md -->
<!-- ============================================================ -->

# VoIP and Communications Acquisition Brief

**KaNeXT LLC | Confidential**

---

## Overview

KaNeXT is acquiring an operating VoIP company to power the communications layer of the OS. The acquisition provides existing telecommunications infrastructure, an established customer base producing $3.2M-$4.4M in annual recurring revenue, multi-number architecture, domestic and international call routing, number provisioning at scale, SIP trunking for PSTN connectivity, and WebRTC infrastructure for in-app calling.

The VoIP layer is not designed as a standalone profit center. It is a user acquisition and retention engine. Every user who receives a KaNeXT phone number is also onboarded into the wallet layer (generating float income), has access to content on KTV (generating advertising revenue), and can participate in commerce (generating processing revenue). The phone number is the entry point. The economic value is in everything the user does after receiving it.

Budget allocation: $15M. Existing recurring revenue continues post-acquisition and offsets operating costs while KaNeXT scales the user base. Acquisition compresses years of telecom infrastructure buildout, regulatory setup, and carrier interconnection into one move while providing an immediate revenue-generating customer base.

---

## The Identity Model

Every KaNeXT user holds one account with one username, which is their universal identifier across the platform. Inside the app, users communicate by username. Messaging, calling, and payments all route through the username. Most users will never type a phone number inside KaNeXT. They search for a name or username the same way they would on any messaging platform.

Phone numbers exist for external connectivity. Each institutional affiliation a user holds can carry its own number: a campus number for a student, a coaching number for a coach, a ministry number for a pastor. These are real phone numbers that receive calls from any phone worldwide. Numbers are assigned automatically when a user joins an institution. No setup, no selection, no configuration.

The purpose is institutional separation. A coach's recruiting calls to prospects are on the coaching number: auditable, compliant, documented. Personal calls stay on the personal number. A pastor's ministry communication stays on the ministry number. These contexts never cross unless the user bridges them intentionally. All communication within each institutional context is governed by RBAC and fully auditable. This serves both privacy and institutional governance. The governance dimension is the more structurally significant: all communication within each institutional context is governed by RBAC and fully auditable.

The username also serves as the payment address. Sending money to a KaNeXT username delivers funds directly to that user's wallet. One identity for communication and finance.

---

## Institutional Communication

The highest-frequency use of the communication layer is not international calling. It is daily communication inside institutions.

Every institution that deploys KaNeXT replaces fragmented communication tools (separate email systems, separate phone infrastructure, separate team messaging apps, separate group chats) with one governed platform.

At a school network institution: coaches message the team, coordinate with athletic trainers, and contact recruits through the same system. Faculty communicate with students through advising threads. Administrative staff coordinate across departments. The athletic director messages all coaches simultaneously. Recruiting coordinators contact prospects through compliant, documented channels with no personal numbers, no unmonitored texts, and no compliance risk.

At mandate institutions: coaches who currently use personal phones, group texts, and social media DMs to coordinate with players and recruits shift to governed communication inside the app. Opponent scouting, game preparation, travel coordination, and eligibility communication all happen in one place.

At faith institutions: ministry leaders coordinate with volunteers, pastoral care teams communicate with members, and cross-campus church networks connect globally, all inside the app, all governed, all auditable.

This is the daily utility that makes the platform sticky. A user who makes one international call per week but sends 50 messages per day inside their institution is retained by the internal communication, not the international calling.

---

## The Diaspora Play

Free on-network international calling is the mechanism that converts diaspora families into platform users.

Large Nigerian-American, Caribbean, Latin American, and West African diaspora communities are concentrated across major U.S. metros. These communities currently rely on WhatsApp for communication and Western Union, MoneyGram, or similar services for remittance.

KaNeXT offers a structurally different alternative. Free on-network international calls replace WhatsApp for voice communication, with the addition of institutional identities, a wallet, streaming, and commerce that WhatsApp does not provide. Competitive remittance pricing (targeting a fraction of the industry average cost) replaces traditional remittance providers.

The acquisition path is organic. A student at a mandate institution or network school calls home for free. The parent downloads the app to receive the call. The parent now has a wallet and can send or receive remittance at competitive rates. They tell family members. Family members download the app. The network grows through existing social connections without paid marketing.

---

## Call Types and Economics

**On-network (KaNeXT to KaNeXT):** Free. Server-to-server data packets. Negligible bandwidth cost. Works globally. This is the core value proposition.

**Off-network outbound (KaNeXT to regular phone):** $0.01-$0.03 per minute via VoIP provider infrastructure. Can be absorbed as a user acquisition cost or passed through depending on volume and strategic priority.

**Off-network inbound (regular phone to KaNeXT):** Supported. KaNeXT numbers are real phone numbers that receive calls from any phone worldwide.

**Number provisioning cost:** Approximately $1 per month per number at standard rates, declining with enterprise volume. At scale, this cost is offset by wallet float, commerce processing, and remittance revenue generated by each user.

---

## Strategic Value

The VoIP layer transforms KaNeXT from an institutional operating system into a global communications platform. One free phone call creates a platform user generating revenue across multiple channels: wallet float, commerce processing, KTV advertising, and remittance.

The diaspora play acquires users. The institutional communication retains them. The wallet monetizes them. The communication layer is a user-acquisition engine with low marginal cost at scale.

Revenue model details are documented separately.

# Enrollment Engine Brief

**KaNeXT LLC | Confidential**

---

## Overview

The enrollment engine is the conversion layer between platform activity, athletic visibility, and tuition revenue. It is the function that takes the awareness KaNeXT creates (athletics, media, diaspora, intelligence-identified prospects) and converts it into enrolled students paying tuition.

Year 1 operates under the Athletics-Only Agreement. Enrollment engine scope is athletic recruitment (finding and converting the athletes who fill the 12-team Varsity and Development roster structure) plus the institutional enrollment channels directly activated by athletic visibility. Year 2 activates Full IOA and expands the enrollment engine to institution-wide operations covering residential and online enrollment across the partner institution's program portfolio.

Year 1 enrollment infrastructure is built within KaNeXT Core Operations. Professional enrollment staff, CRM platform, digital marketing infrastructure, and intelligence-driven lead scoring are deployed at Year 1 scale. An enrollment company acquisition is a Year 2+ strategic capital event to accelerate capacity, team scale, and call center operations for the institution-wide enrollment expansion described in the revenue model.

---

## Why This Matters: Comparable Institutional Growth

Grand Canyon University grew from fewer than 1,000 on-campus students in 2008 to approximately 25,000 on-campus and 98,000 online by 2024. Total enrollment exceeds 125,000 after $1.8B in campus investment over 16 years.

Liberty University grew to over 140,000 students by 2024, including approximately 16,000 residential and 124,000 online. Annual revenue reached $1.2B with $3.5B in net assets.

High Point University grew from 1,450 students in 2005 to 6,335 on-campus by 2024 with over $3B in total campus investment.

These institutions achieved this growth with none of the structural advantages KaNeXT provides. No intelligence system identifying prospects. No KTV generating millions of views. No VoIP funneling diaspora families into the app. No mandate creating platform awareness across hundreds of schools. No owned media surface. No integrated enrollment-to-tuition-to-wallet pipeline. No multi-sport athletic system producing hundreds of games per year on a nationally visible platform.

They grew through enrollment marketing, campus investment, and institutional brand building. The partner institution will have all of those traditional growth drivers plus an integrated institutional operating system that may meaningfully improve conversion economics and reduce customer acquisition costs relative to traditional enrollment marketing.

---

## Why an Enrollment Engine Is Required

The revenue model projects total enrollment growing from 2,000-2,200 in Year 1 to 24,600-27,700 by Year 5. Online enrollment grows from 400-500 in Year 1 to 11,800-13,500 by Year 5. Residential enrollment grows from 1,500-1,700 in Year 1 to 12,800-14,200 by Year 5. Neither trajectory is achievable through passive admissions. Both require a professional enrollment operation converting the awareness and demand that the rest of the system generates.

The partner institution currently operates at approximately 1,100 enrollment. The institution has existing enrollment operations tied to its ABHE-accredited programs but lacks the marketing infrastructure, call center scale, digital lead generation, and conversion pipeline required to support multi-year enrollment scaling at the trajectory the partnership projects. The enrollment engine creates this capability within the partnership while respecting the partner institution's institutional identity and mission.

---

## The Structural Advantages

**The awareness already exists.** The enrollment engine does not build awareness from scratch. It converts awareness that the rest of the ecosystem is already generating. The partner institution's 12 Varsity and Development teams produce 250-350+ games per year on KTV visible to every user on the platform. D1 guarantee games against Division I opponents generate national media exposure. The philanthropic commitment and campus transformation generate national press. Mandate institution games (tens of thousands annually across GAAC, NAIA, USCAA, NCCAA member schools) create platform-wide content that surfaces the partner institution brand. Diaspora families connected through VoIP and remittance are already inside the app. The enrollment engine's primary job is not advertising. It is converting the demand that every other part of the system produces.

**Intelligence-driven targeting.** The enrollment engine has access to data no typical enrollment company has. The intelligence system identifies athletes in the national player pool across every mandate institution, each one a potential recruit for the partner institution. The platform tracks which families are on KTV, which diaspora communities are connected through VoIP, which prospects have engaged with the partner institution content, and which users have wallets and financial activity indicating institutional affinity. The CRM does not start with a purchased lead list. It starts with a growing ecosystem audience. The Enrollment Intelligence architecture (RKR Retention, PKR Persistence, PLKR Pipeline, RCKR Recovery) governs lead scoring, persistence modeling, and pipeline management across the funnel.

**Institutional integration.** A prospect who fills out an interest form can immediately be contacted through KaNeXT messaging, scheduled through Dipson, shown a campus tour on KTV, connected with a current student through the app, and processed for financial aid through KPay, all without leaving the ecosystem. The enrollment funnel is not a separate website feeding a separate CRM. It is a native function of the platform the prospect is already using.

**Dipson assists at every stage.** Prospects can ask Dipson questions about programs, campus life, financial aid, and application requirements at any hour. Dipson responds within the authority scope appropriate for prospective students. Admissions counselors use Dipson to pull prospect data, generate personalized outreach, and manage pipeline workflows.

**The evangelical Christian identity.** the partner institution's identity as an ABHE-accredited evangelical Christian institution is marketable to distinct audiences: Christian-identifying families seeking faith-integrated higher education, international students from Christian communities worldwide (the partner institution already serves 424 international students from 43 countries), working professionals seeking values-aligned online education, and pastors and ministry leaders pursuing theological and leadership credentials. These are large, underserved audiences with strong institutional loyalty when the right school reaches them.

---

## How the Enrollment Engine Works

**Digital marketing** drives awareness where the ecosystem has not already created it: targeted advertising, search engine optimization, content marketing, and social media campaigns positioning the partner institution to prospective students nationally and internationally. The digital marketing function scales significantly under Full IOA activation in Year 2.

**The call center** converts leads. Admissions counselors contact prospects, answer questions, guide applications, and close enrollments. A prospective student who engages with the platform, whether through a game on KTV, a social media ad, a referral from a mandate institution coach, or a family connection through VoIP, receives follow-up measured in hours rather than traditional institutional timelines.

**The CRM** manages the pipeline. Every prospect is tracked from first touch through enrollment. Lead source attribution shows which channels produce enrolled students, not just clicks. The intelligence system scores prospects on institutional fit, academic readiness, and engagement level, prioritizing counselor time on the highest-probability conversions.

**Year 1 scope:** athletic enrollment (180-220 athletes across 12 teams), general institutional enrollment driven by platform visibility, and pipeline buildout for Year 2 scale expansion. Year 2 scope: full institution-wide enrollment operations across residential and online programs under the Full IOA structure.

---

## The Scale Opportunity

GCU, Liberty, and High Point demonstrated that aggressive enrollment marketing combined with campus investment and institutional brand building can produce massive growth at private universities. Each of those schools started with less capital, less technology, and less structural advantage than the partner institution will have under the partnership.

The partner institution's Year 5 projection of 24,600-27,700 total enrollment (12,800-14,200 residential plus 11,800-13,500 online) represents a fraction of the scale GCU and Liberty achieved over longer time horizons with less initial capital and fewer integrated growth engines. The projection is aggressive but grounded in demonstrated institutional growth precedents and KaNeXT's integrated operating model. Actual results depend on execution, program approvals, faculty scaling, conversion rates, ABHE compliance through the Full IOA substantive change process, and market conditions.

---

## Enrollment Company Acquisition (Year 2+ Capital Event)

The enrollment company acquisition is a Year 2+ capital event raised separately from the Year 1 partnership funding. The acquisition target is a higher education enrollment marketing firm providing 20-40 employees, an established call center operation, CRM and digital marketing infrastructure, admissions counselor teams, and lead generation and conversion pipelines.

Post-acquisition, the operation is dedicated to the partner institution and KaNeXT exclusively. Every counselor hour, every CRM workflow, every marketing dollar is directed at the partner institution enrollment and KaNeXT institutional growth. No competing clients. No split attention. Acquisition compresses years of enrollment infrastructure buildout, team recruitment, and operational refinement into one move while providing an immediately functional operation for the Year 2-5 enrollment scale expansion.

The acquisition is sized and funded against Year 1 the partner institution execution and the scale expansion triggered by Full IOA activation. Budget and structure are determined at the time of the capital event.

---

## Strategic Value

The enrollment engine is the conversion layer between platform activity and tuition revenue. KTV creates visibility. VoIP creates diaspora connections. Athletics creates brand awareness. The intelligence system identifies prospects. The enrollment engine converts all of it into enrolled students paying tuition through KPay.

Without the enrollment engine, the awareness generated by the rest of the system dissipates. With it, every KTV viewer, every VoIP user, every mandate institution parent, and every athlete in the national database is a prospect in a governed pipeline.

Revenue model details are documented separately in the Institutional Revenue Model and Enrollment Funnel and Conversion documents.

# Fulfillment Center Brief

**KaNeXT LLC | Confidential**

---

## Overview

KaNeXT is building an in-house fulfillment operation for merchandise production, warehousing, and distribution across the entire platform. Every mandate institution, every school network institution, every athlete NIL store, every brand store across all five modes, and every KaNeXT-owned event is served by a single production operation.

The fulfillment center is not a third-party print shop. It is owned infrastructure that produces merch on demand, ships directly, and settles every transaction through KPay's governed settlement chain. Every order is a commerce event that generates 5% processing revenue for KaNeXT in addition to production margin and revenue share.

Budget allocation: $5M.

---

## The Product Tiers

**Tier 1 - Standard.** Print-on-demand merchandise available to every mandate institution and brand on the platform at zero setup cost. T-shirts, hoodies, hats, and accessories with school or brand identity. A fan orders a hoodie. The order routes to the fulfillment center. The hoodie is produced, packed, and shipped. No inventory risk for the school or brand.

**Tier 2 - Premium.** Higher quality blanks, expanded product selection, and custom design options for schools and brands with growing demand. Still print-on-demand for most items, with pre-produced inventory on proven sellers to improve quality and reduce per-unit cost.

**Tier 3 - Designer and Exclusive.** Limited-edition drops with premium materials and packaging. Athlete-designed collections. Conference championship gear. Event-specific merchandise. Higher retail prices and higher margins at every level.

---

## Unit Economics

Illustrative example: a $45 hoodie (Tier 1 or Tier 2). Approximately $25 COGS (blank, printing, packaging, shipping). Approximately $12 to the school or brand (revenue share). Approximately $6 KaNeXT production margin. Plus $2.25 in 5% commerce processing on the transaction through KPay.

Tier 3 products carry higher retail prices and proportionally higher margins. A $95 limited-edition hoodie with premium blank and designer graphics generates more margin at every level: production, revenue share, and processing.

At scale, per-unit costs decrease across all tiers. Bulk blank purchasing reduces material cost. Pre-produced items reduce per-unit production cost. The fixed cost base is shared across every storefront on the platform. Margin improves with every brand added and every order fulfilled.

---

## Phased Scaling

**Phase 1: Miami Hub.** Located in the Miami metro area. Handles all production and shipping for the initial mandate rollout, school network institutions, and early brand stores. Equipment: commercial-grade DTG printers, heat presses, embroidery machine, vinyl cutter, shipping station, and initial blank inventory. Setup cost: $95,000-$150,000. Staffing: 4-5 full-time and 1-2 part-time employees. Target capacity: 500-1,000 orders per week scaling to 2,000+ as adoption grows.

**Phase 2: Central US Hub.** Once order volume exceeds 3,000 per week, a second fulfillment center opens in a central US location to reduce average shipping times to 2-4 days for the majority of the country. Mirrors Miami equipment and staffing.

**Phase 3: Pre-Produced and Hybrid Model.** For schools, athletes, and brands with proven sales volume, transition from pure print-on-demand to a hybrid model. Pre-produced inventory on top sellers improves quality, reduces per-unit cost by 20-30%, and enables same-day shipping.

---

## Who It Serves

**Mandate institutions (1,000+ at full deployment).** Each receives a managed Tier 1 merch store at zero cost as part of the mandate package. The school's branding is applied to standard product templates. Revenue share flows back to the school through KPay.

**School network institutions.** Full Tier 1-3 product access. Uniforms, game-day merchandise, campus bookstore inventory, alumni merchandise, and event-specific products.

**Athlete NIL stores.** Individual athletes across the platform can sell branded merchandise through their Personal mode Earn tile. Production and fulfillment are handled by the center. The athlete receives revenue share. KaNeXT earns production margin and commerce processing.

**Brand stores across all modes.** A church sells ministry-branded merchandise. A business sells branded products. A creator sells merch through their Personal brand. Every storefront across every mode is a potential fulfillment customer.

**KaNeXT-owned events.** Tournament merchandise, showcase event gear, and conference championship products are all produced and fulfilled by the same operation.

---

## Strategic Value

The fulfillment center turns every brand on the platform into a merch store without the brand needing to source products, manage inventory, or handle shipping. The school does nothing. The athlete does nothing. The church does nothing. They choose designs, set prices, and the fulfillment center handles everything else.

At 1,000+ mandate institutions plus network institutions plus athlete NIL stores plus brand stores across all modes plus tournament properties, the fulfillment center serves thousands of storefronts through a single production operation. The fixed cost base is shared across every storefront. Each brand added increases volume without proportionally increasing cost.

Every transaction flows through KPay. Every order is a governed commerce event. The fulfillment center is the physical goods layer that turns the platform's commerce architecture into tangible products shipped to real people.

Revenue model details are documented separately.

<!-- FILE: 08_Regulatory_and_Compliance/regulatory_accreditation_implementation.md -->

# Regulatory and Accreditation Implementation Memo

**KaNeXT LLC | Confidential**

---

## Purpose

This document maps the regulatory and accreditation workstreams required to execute the KaNeXT partnership with SFBC. It is a practical implementation guide, not a legal opinion. Each workstream identifies what must happen, in what sequence, with what regulatory body, and on what approximate timeline.

The key strategic insight: Year 1 operates as an Athletics-Only Agreement that does not trigger substantive change review. KaNeXT deploys capital, operates athletic programs, builds infrastructure, and generates visibility from Day 1 without regulatory dependency. Regulatory workstreams activate in preparation for Year 2 Full IOA expansion and NAIA Varsity activation, not as blockers to Year 1 deployment.

---

## Workstream 1: ABHE Accreditation

The Association for Biblical Higher Education (ABHE) is SFBC's institutional accreditor. ABHE is a USDE-recognized national specialized institutional accrediting agency, recognized by CHEA as a national, faith-related accrediting agency. Accreditation is the regulatory asset that enables Title IV eligibility, institutional legitimacy, and degree-granting authority.

### Current Status

SFBC's ABHE accreditation is in good standing. Probation was lifted in 2024 following institutional improvements. The next reaffirmation cycle is 2028. Accreditation covers undergraduate certificates, associate degrees, bachelor's degrees, graduate certificates, and master's degrees. Doctoral programs are outside ABHE's USDE-recognized scope and are not part of the Year 1 through Year 5 partnership scope.

ABHE requires annual affirmation of the Tenets of Faith by the Chief Executive Officer, institutional mission alignment with biblical higher education, an external governing board of at least five members (excluding all employees except the CEO), nonprofit status, and ongoing compliance with Conditions of Eligibility and Standards for Accreditation.

The KaNeXT partnership structure preserves every one of these requirements. The Athletics-Only Agreement in Year 1 and the Full IOA in Year 2 separate academic authority (SFBC retains) from operational partnership (KaNeXT provides). The institutional mission, Tenets of Faith affirmation, board governance, and nonprofit status all remain SFBC's responsibility and operate independently of the KaNeXT operational role.

### Year 1 Athletics-Only Agreement: No Substantive Change

The Year 1 Athletics-Only Agreement does not constitute a substantive change under ABHE policy. KaNeXT operates athletic programs autonomously. SFBC retains full academic authority, institutional governance, and institutional control. The arrangement is a services partnership structured to remain within ordinary vendor and service boundaries, similar to how institutions contract with athletic management firms, enrollment marketing companies, or technology vendors.

No ABHE substantive change proposal is required for Year 1 deployment. Athletic programs launch, enrollment operations activate, platform infrastructure deploys, and the partnership operates without ABHE pre-approval because no substantive change threshold is crossed.

### Year 2 Full IOA: Substantive Change Process

The Full IOA activation in Year 2 changes the scope of partnership from athletics-only to institutional operating partnership. This constitutes a substantive change requiring ABHE review and approval.

**Process Map:**

1. **Pre-consultation with ABHE Substantive Change Officer.** ABHE policy requires consultation at least 6 months before intended implementation. KaNeXT and SFBC engage the Substantive Change Officer during Year 1 to preview the IOA structure, understand ABHE's expectations, identify potential concerns, and shape the formal submission.

2. **Board approval of Full IOA.** The SFBC Board of Trustees formally approves the Full IOA. Board approval is a prerequisite for the ABHE substantive change submission.

3. **Substantive change proposal submission.** Formal proposal to ABHE detailing the Full IOA structure, governance safeguards, financial projections, academic authority preservation, institutional mission continuity, and compliance with Conditions of Eligibility and Standards for Accreditation. The proposal must demonstrate that the partnership preserves biblical higher education mission, Tenets of Faith affirmation, external board governance, nonprofit status, and academic quality.

4. **ABHE review and decision.** Substantive changes reviewed by the Committee on Progress Reports and Substantive Change (PRSC) require COA approval. Timelines per ABHE policy: 90 days for Substantive Change Officer decisions, up to 180 days for COA-referred decisions. Certain substantive changes may require a focused visit for verification.

### Miami Lakes Additional Location: Separate Substantive Change

The Miami Lakes campus acquisition and operational activation constitutes a separate ABHE substantive change for additional location. This process runs independently from the Full IOA substantive change and is triggered when Miami Lakes becomes operational.

Miami Lakes Phase 1 is projected for Year 3 operational launch. The additional location substantive change proposal is submitted in advance of operational launch with appropriate lead time per ABHE consultation requirements. Main campus relocation, if pursued in a later year, would constitute a separate substantive change at that time.

### Online Program Expansion Thresholds

ABHE substantive change thresholds related to distance education:
- First offering of courses using a new instructional modality up to 49% of a program requires substantive change approval
- Reaching the 50% threshold for distance education (first credential completable 50%+ online, or 50%+ of students enrolled in distance education) requires substantive change approval
- Additional programs, new delivery modalities, or new degree levels within ABHE's USDE-recognized scope require substantive change approval

Online program expansion proceeds through the standard ABHE substantive change process as each threshold is approached. SFBC's existing online programs and recent additions (MBA launched Fall 2025, BSN in development) are existing or in-process expansions. Further program expansion operates within the same framework.

### 2028 Reaffirmation Cycle

SFBC's ABHE reaffirmation is scheduled for 2028, which falls within Year 2 of the partnership. The Full IOA substantive change process and the reaffirmation cycle may overlap. The partnership is designed to strengthen reaffirmation posture by demonstrating financial sustainability, operational capacity, institutional investment in quality, and compliance with Conditions of Eligibility and Standards for Accreditation. Approaching reaffirmation with a funded operating partnership, capital commitment, and demonstrated institutional transformation positions SFBC materially stronger than approaching reaffirmation with existing standalone resources.

### Timeline: ABHE workstream

- Year 1: Pre-consultation with Substantive Change Officer, board engagement, proposal preparation
- Year 2: Formal substantive change submission, review period, decision
- Year 2 onward: Full IOA activation upon approval; ongoing substantive change submissions for additional locations, program expansion, and reaffirmation activities

---

## Workstream 2: Title IV Federal Financial Aid

SFBC participates in Title IV programs (Pell Grants, federal student loans). Title IV eligibility is essential for institutional viability.

### Year 1: No CIO Trigger

The Athletics-Only Agreement does not constitute a change in operational control or ownership. SFBC retains institutional control. No change-in-control (CIO) review by the Department of Education is triggered. Title IV participation continues without interruption.

### Year 2 Full IOA: CIO Review

Full IOA activation constitutes a material change in how the institution is operated. The DOE evaluates whether the institution remains eligible for Title IV participation under the new structure.

**Process Map:**

1. **Pre-CIO engagement.** Concurrent with the ABHE pre-consultation in Year 1, notify the DOE of the anticipated change. Engage legal counsel experienced in Title IV CIO processes.

2. **CIO application.** Submit the formal change-in-control application to the DOE. The application includes the IOA, governance documentation, financial projections, and evidence of institutional capacity. The DOE evaluates financial responsibility, administrative capability, and compliance history.

3. **Provisional Program Participation Agreement (PPA).** The DOE may issue a provisional PPA during the review period, allowing Title IV participation to continue while the full review is conducted. Provisional PPAs are common in CIO transactions but are not guaranteed. Legal counsel engages DOE early to clarify the provisional participation path.

4. **Full DOE review and determination.** The DOE reviews the structure against Title IV requirements. Key considerations: whether the operating agreement benefits the institution rather than disproportionately benefiting the provider, whether governance safeguards are adequate, whether financial responsibility standards are met, whether the institution's nonprofit status is maintained for Title IV purposes. Outcomes depend on the DOE's assessment of the specific facts.

### Relevant Precedent

The Grand Canyon University Ninth Circuit ruling (November 2024) and DOE recognition developments (December 2025) provide useful context for how for-profit service provider structures have been evaluated under Title IV. The KaNeXT IOA is designed to address DOE concerns that have historically emerged in operator-partner arrangements: governance safeguards include independent oversight, the revenue structure reflects institutional benefit, structural separation of roles avoids dual-CEO issues, and the partnership preserves nonprofit status for Title IV purposes. Each institution's CIO review proceeds on its own specific facts, not solely on precedent.

### Timeline: 6-18 months, largely concurrent with ABHE substantive change process.

---

## Workstream 3: IRS and Nonprofit Status

SFBC is a 501(c)(3) tax-exempt institution. Maintaining this status is essential for receiving tax-deductible philanthropic contributions, preserving Title IV eligibility, and qualifying for various federal and state programs.

### What Must Be Maintained

The operating relationship must satisfy the IRS operational test: the nonprofit must be primarily operated for tax-exempt educational purposes. The IOA is structured to satisfy this test. SFBC retains full academic governance. Tuition revenue allocation ensures the majority flows to educational purposes. The institutional mission and identity are preserved. No private benefit or inurement occurs.

### Process Map

1. **Legal review of IOA against IRS standards.** Tax counsel reviews the IOA before execution to confirm compliance with 501(c)(3) operational test requirements. The GCU precedent is directly relevant: GCU maintained IRS tax-exempt status throughout the DOE dispute, validating that operator-partner structures do not inherently compromise nonprofit status.

2. **Private benefit and inurement analysis.** Counsel confirms the IOA does not result in private inurement. The independent fairness opinion required by the IOA addresses this directly.

3. **IRS notification if required.** Counsel determines whether a new IRS determination letter is required or whether the existing 501(c)(3) status carries forward. In most cases involving a services agreement rather than a change in corporate form, the existing status carries forward.

### Timeline: 2-4 months for legal review, concurrent with other workstreams.

---

## Workstream 4: NAIA Membership Application

The lead athletic workstream. SFBC is not currently an NAIA member institution. The NAIA application cycle operates twice yearly: applications by April 15 receive decisions in October; applications by October 15 receive decisions in April. Active membership typically begins the fall following the decision.

### Current Status

SFBC's existing athletic programs (men's basketball and men's soccer, launched Fall 2025) compete in the Gulf-Atlantic Athletic Conference (GAAC), of which SFBC is a charter member. No NAIA membership exists today.

### Application Timeline

- **October 15, 2026:** KaNeXT and SFBC submit NAIA membership application
- **April 2027:** NAIA Council of Presidents decision
- **Fall 2027 (Year 2 of partnership):** Active NAIA membership begins

The 6 Varsity programs planned for NAIA Varsity competition are built Year 1 with infrastructure, coaching, recruiting, and roster development ready for Year 2 NAIA activation. Year 1 competitive affiliation for these 6 Varsity programs is selected from available options: USCAA membership, NCCAA affiliation, independent scheduling, or combination thereof. GAAC continues to serve as a conference home for any additional teams beyond the 6 Varsity core.

### NAIA Application Requirements

- Financial stability demonstrated through institutional audits and capital commitment
- Sports sponsorship meeting NAIA minimum sport count requirements
- Institutional accreditation (SFBC's ABHE accreditation satisfies this)
- Commitment to character-driven athletics per NAIA Champions of Character framework
- Athletics facilities appropriate to sport sponsorship
- Enrollment and retention data supporting competitive participation
- Overall athletics budget adequate to support the sponsored sports

KaNeXT's capital commitment, the institutional investment in athletic facilities and infrastructure, the recruited roster quality, and the coaching staff assembled under the partnership strengthen the application substantially beyond what SFBC could present as a standalone applicant.

### Title IX Compliance

NAIA Varsity program launch requires Title IX compliance across the sport portfolio. Gender equity in participation opportunities, athletic financial aid, and other program support must be demonstrable. The Year 1 buildout is designed with Title IX compliance in mind for the 6 Varsity sports, with gender balance across the sport selection and proportional financial support.

### Timeline: NAIA workstream

- **October 15, 2026:** Application submission
- **October 2026 through April 2027:** NAIA review process, potential site visits, committee interviews
- **April 2027:** NAIA Council of Presidents decision
- **July 1, 2027:** Coaches complete NAIA Champions of Character coaching course within 60 days of active membership
- **Fall 2027:** Active NAIA membership, Varsity competition begins

---

## Workstream 5: Online Program Expansion

The Full IOA gives KaNeXT authority to develop and scale online programs. New online degree programs require academic governance approval from SFBC and ABHE substantive change approval.

### Process Map

1. **Identify expansion programs.** Working with SFBC academic leadership, identify which existing accredited programs can be offered online and which new programs should be developed.

2. **Faculty and curriculum governance.** Each new online program is approved through SFBC's academic governance process (faculty committee, provost, board as required). KaNeXT provides the delivery infrastructure. SFBC provides the academic approval.

3. **ABHE substantive change submission.** New programs or new delivery modalities require ABHE substantive change approval. Online expansion of existing programs or new degree programs submit through the standard substantive change process.

4. **Title IV program eligibility.** New programs may require separate Title IV eligibility determination. Programs offered entirely online must comply with state authorization requirements in every state where students are enrolled.

### Timeline: 6-12 months per program. Online expansion sequences across Year 1 preparation and Year 2 onward execution.

---

## Workstream 6: State of Florida

SFBC operates under Florida law. The partnership structure must comply with Florida nonprofit governance requirements, state higher education regulations, and state-level authorization requirements.

### Key Considerations

- Florida Commission for Independent Education oversight (applicable to independent institutions)
- Florida nonprofit corporation law compliance
- State authorization for online programs - Florida's SARA participation (State Authorization Reciprocity Agreement) simplifies multi-state online authorization
- Miami Lakes campus additional location authorization at the state level, in addition to the ABHE additional location substantive change

### Timeline: Concurrent with other workstreams. No separate extended approval process anticipated unless counsel identifies state-specific requirements beyond standard compliance.

---

## Phased Deployment Strategy

The regulatory timeline does not create an operational delay. KaNeXT deploys operationally from Day 1 through a phased structure that separates Year 1 vendor and services operations (no substantive change triggered) from Year 2 Full IOA activation (requires ABHE and DOE approvals).

### Phase 1: Year 1 Athletics-Only and Services Period

During Year 1, KaNeXT operates through structured institutional agreements that remain within vendor and service boundaries:

**Technology vendor agreement.** KaNeXT OS, KTV, KPay, Dipson, KVision, KStat, KDraw, VoIP, and platform infrastructure deploy as institutional technology services.

**Athletic management agreement (Athletics-Only Agreement).** KaNeXT operates athletic programs autonomously as an external services partner. Coaching staff are hired under this framework with KaNeXT funding. 6 Varsity programs built NAIA-ready.

**Enrollment services partnership.** The enrollment engine operates as a student acquisition and marketing partner. Enrollment services at athletics scope Year 1, institution-wide scope Year 2 upon Full IOA activation.

**Media and infrastructure services.** Camera deployment, KTV streaming setup, KStat integration, and KVision processing deploy as media vendor services.

During Phase 1, KaNeXT funds all deployments as capital investment. The partner institution operates normally with new service providers. Institutional revenue continues under the partner institution's existing structure. The Year 1 athletic season launches. Enrollment marketing activates. Games are filmed and streamed. The OS runs. Intelligence is operational. Infrastructure builds. The institution transforms in practice while the Year 2 governance change processes through ABHE and DOE in parallel.

### Phase 2: Year 2 Full IOA Activation

Upon ABHE substantive change approval and DOE CIO determination, the Full IOA executes. Revenue allocation under IOA terms activates. Governance provisions take effect. Operational authority formally transfers to KaNeXT under IOA terms. NAIA Varsity membership activates concurrently. The 6 Varsity programs transition from Year 1 competitive affiliation to NAIA Varsity.

### Back-Payment Reconciliation

If permitted by counsel and regulatorily supportable, the IOA may include a retroactive reconciliation provision covering the Year 1 vendor and services period. Upon IOA activation, the revenue allocation may be reconciled as if IOA terms had been in effect from Year 1 launch. KaNeXT would be made whole for capital deployed during Year 1 through a pre-agreed commercial term of the IOA activated upon formal approval.

This mechanism requires legal structuring to ensure it does not create the appearance that IOA economics were functionally in place before approval. Counsel must confirm compatibility with ABHE standards, Title IV requirements, and nonprofit governance obligations.

### Why This Works

From ABHE's perspective: the institution adopted new technology vendors, athletic services, and enrollment partners in Year 1 (ordinary services), then in Year 2 formalized a deeper operating partnership reviewed and approved through the substantive change process. The phasing demonstrates institutional responsibility by deploying services before executing operational governance change.

From the DOE's perspective: Year 1 operates under existing institutional control, maintaining Title IV eligibility without CIO trigger. Year 2 CIO review proceeds on a prepared application with fully disclosed institutional transformation context.

From the investor's perspective: KaNeXT is fully operational from Day 1. Revenue economics are preserved through the reconciliation provision. The regulatory timeline does not create a Year 1 revenue gap. It creates a Year 1 services period that reconciles into full IOA economics upon Year 2 activation.

### Red-Line Triggers

The phased deployment strategy is monitored for triggers requiring reassessment:

- If the cumulative scope of vendor agreements begins to resemble governance transfer rather than service provision, Year 1 structure must be narrowed until approvals are obtained
- If ABHE informally signals that the phased approach is being viewed as de facto control change, deployment scope must be narrowed to clearly non-control functions
- If academic oversight boundaries blur (KaNeXT staff directing academic decisions, controlling faculty, directing curriculum), structural separation must be reinforced immediately
- If the SFBC board cannot reach approval on the Full IOA due to governance considerations, the Year 2 timeline shifts and Year 1 scope must be reassessed for standalone viability
- If the DOE frames the Year 1 services arrangement as a control event earlier than Full IOA activation, legal counsel must engage DOE immediately to clarify boundaries and potentially accelerate the CIO process
- If NAIA application is deferred or denied, Year 2 competitive affiliation must be adjusted to preserve program continuity while reapplying or pursuing alternative association pathways

These triggers are decision points requiring real-time legal and strategic judgment. The phased strategy is adaptive.

---

## Master Timeline

| Workstream | Start | Duration | Critical Dependencies |
|---|---|---|---|
| **Year 1: Athletics-Only Agreement deployment** | **Partnership execution** | **Year 1 ongoing** | **Athletics Operating Agreement signed** |
| Technology and OS deployment | Year 1 start | 2-4 months | Vendor agreements signed |
| Athletic staff hiring and program launch | Year 1 start | 3-6 months | Athletics Operating Agreement |
| Cluster housing and facilities buildout | Year 1 start | 6-12 months | Lease agreements, construction |
| Enrollment services activation | Year 1 start | 1-3 months | Enrollment partnership signed |
| Media and camera deployment | Year 1 start | 2-4 months | Equipment procurement |
| ABHE pre-consultation | Year 1 early | 2 months | Legal counsel engaged |
| NAIA application preparation | Year 1 | 3-6 months | Athletics buildout underway |
| **NAIA application submission** | **October 15, 2026** | - | **Institutional readiness demonstrated** |
| Board engagement for Full IOA | Year 1 mid | 3-6 months | SFBC board coordination |
| ABHE substantive change submission | Year 1 late / Year 2 early | - | Board approval complete |
| Title IV CIO application | Year 1 late / Year 2 early | - | Board approval complete |
| IRS legal review | Year 1 | 2-4 months | Tax counsel engaged |
| ABHE review and decision | Year 2 | 3-6 months | Submission accepted |
| DOE review and provisional PPA | Year 2 | 6-18 months | CIO application filed |
| **NAIA Council of Presidents decision** | **April 2027** | - | **Application review complete** |
| **Year 2: Full IOA activation** | **Upon ABHE and DOE approvals** | - | **Regulatory approvals complete** |
| NAIA Varsity membership active | Fall 2027 | - | NAIA decision approved |
| Back-payment reconciliation | Upon IOA activation | 30-60 days | IOA executed |
| Miami Lakes additional location approval | Year 2-3 | 6-12 months | Campus acquisition progress |
| Online program expansion (by phase) | Year 1 through Year 5 | Rolling | Academic governance and ABHE approvals |

The critical path runs through ABHE and DOE review of the Full IOA, with NAIA review operating in parallel. KaNeXT is fully operational during the Year 1 review period. The institution transforms from Day 1. Formal governance and association activations complete in Year 2.

---

## Disclaimer

This document is a strategic implementation outline, not legal advice. All regulatory processes, timelines, and outcomes depend on the specific requirements of each regulatory body, legal counsel's guidance, and the institution's compliance posture at the time of submission. Actual timelines may differ materially from estimates. The phased deployment strategy and back-payment reconciliation provision require legal structuring and review to ensure compliance with ABHE standards, Title IV requirements, NAIA membership criteria, and applicable state and federal law. NAIA membership approval is subject to the Council of Presidents decision and is not guaranteed by application submission. The Year 1 competitive affiliation for Varsity programs will be determined based on available association options at the time of Year 1 operational launch.
